BREAKING NEWS: Jeanine Pirro Holds Briefing After Judge Blocks Subpoenas In Jerome Powell Case

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·15:30 ·10.4K views · 2026-03-13
Jeanine Pirro (U.S. Attorney) Jerome Powell (Chair, Federal Reserve)
U.S.
TL;DR U.S. Attorney Jeanine Pirro announced the DOJ will appeal an "activist" ruling by Judge James Boasberg that blocked grand jury subpoenas investigating Federal Reserve Chairman Jerome Powell for $1 billion in renovation cost overruns. Pirro contends the decision creates a...

TL;DR

U.S. Attorney Jeanine Pirro announced the DOJ will appeal an "activist" ruling by Judge James Boasberg that blocked grand jury subpoenas investigating Federal Reserve Chairman Jerome Powell for $1 billion in renovation cost overruns. Pirro contends the decision creates a lawless precedent by allowing a judge to "neuter" the grand jury and shield a public official from standard investigative oversight.

The Investigation into Federal Reserve Misconduct

  • Massive Cost Overruns: The U.S. Attorney’s Office is investigating more than $1 billion in cost overruns regarding renovations to the Federal Reserve headquarters.
  • Questionable Testimony: The inquiry stems from Jerome Powell’s June 2025 testimony before the Senate Banking Committee.
      • Pirro alleges Powell’s statements did not align with publicly available documents regarding the renovation budget.
  • Potential Charges: The DOJ is reviewing evidence related to:
      • 18 U.S.C. § 1001: False statements to federal authorities.
      • Fraud: Statutes related to the misuse of public funds.

Judicial Obstruction and the Boasberg Ruling

  • Subpoenas Quashed: District Court Judge James Boasberg quashed two grand jury subpoenas, preventing the DOJ from reviewing records or presenting them to a grand jury.
  • Allegations of Bias: Pirro labeled Boasberg an "activist judge," claiming his written decision displayed "antipathy" toward the current administration and President Trump.
  • "Bathing in Immunity": Pirro argued the ruling effectively grants Jerome Powell immunity by preventing the grand jury from even hearing evidence, let alone deciding on an indictment.
  • Procedural Errors: The DOJ intends to file a motion for reconsideration because the judge reportedly "got his dates wrong" regarding the timeline of the investigation.

Legal Standards and Grand Jury Authority

  • Departure from Precedent: Pirro asserted that Boasberg is incorrectly requiring the government to show "probable cause" to justify a subpoena.
      • The Correct Standard: According to the Supreme Court, a grand jury can investigate based "merely on suspicion" or a desire for "assurance that [the law] is not" being violated.
  • Dangerous Precedent: The U.S. Attorney warned that this ruling creates a "legal lane" for any defendant to evade investigation by claiming they are a victim of political targeting.
  • Role of the Grand Jury: Pirro emphasized that the grand jury—comprised of ordinary citizens—is the only body that should decide whether to indict or issue a "no true bill" based on the evidence.

Evasion and Political "White Noise"

  • Failure to Cooperate: Pirro detailed multiple attempts to contact the Federal Reserve in December and January to discuss concerns, all of which were ignored.
  • The Powell Response: Rather than complying with subpoenas, Powell released a "woe-is-me" video claiming he was being threatened with indictment.
      • Pirro accused Powell of "ginning up support" from political allies to avoid producing simple documents.
  • Non-Political Mandate: Despite questions regarding the political nature of the case, Pirro maintained her office is focused solely on the law and the "black hole" of public money in D.C.
"Jerome Powell today is now bathed in immunity, preventing my office from investigating the Federal Reserve. This is wrong and it is without legal authority." — Jeanine Pirro
"A grand jury... has broad discretion to investigate merely on the suspicion that the law is being violated... and yet this judge is shockingly requiring the government to show something akin to probable cause." — Jeanine Pirro
"I’ll deal with the devil if you can give me information that will lead me to possibly find a crime. It doesn’t matter where a case comes from. It doesn’t matter." — Jeanine Pirro
No, I'm here to speak about the decision that has been rendered in the case involving the Board of Governors of the Federal Reserve System. Now, the American public is fed up with public monies that seem to go into a black hole, especially in DC, where no one is held accountable. One of the age-old tools that all prosecutors have to investigate any crime, including cost overruns, is a grand jury subpoena. Today, however, in Washington, an activist judge has taken that tool away from us. By inserting himself and preventing the grand jury from even obtaining, let alone hearing evidence, he has neutered the grand jury's ability to investigate crime. As a result, Jerome Powell today is now bathed in immunity, preventing my office from investigating the Federal Reserve. This is wrong and it is without legal authority. In June of 2025, Jerome Powell testified before the Senate Banking Committee, making questionable statements that did not comport with publicly available documents. That was regarding the atrocious cost overrun of more than one billion dollars. I didn't say million; I said billion in renovations to his headquarters. This is from the man who says that he is the steward of our public funds. In November, the United States Attorney's Office began an inquiry. Prosecutors from my office gathered information for months before we served two grand jury subpoenas. On December 19th, we sent an email to the Federal Reserve to have a conversation, a meeting, or even a phone call to discuss our concerns. There was no response. On December 29th, we sent another email to have a conversation, a meeting, or even a phone call to discuss our concerns. Again, we were ignored. We in fact asked to meet the first week in January, and we were ignored. It was at that point that two grand jury subpoenas were issued to the Federal Reserve, not even to Jerome Powell personally. Again, there was no response and no compliance. Instead, we saw a woe-is-me video by Mr. Powell, falsely claiming that he was being threatened with criminal indictment. Claiming victim status, Powell proceeded to call his political friends in DC and around the world to gin up support for himself, all the while refusing to produce simple documents. Now enter local District Court Judge James Boasberg, whose written decision on its face makes clear his antipathy toward President Trump and this administration. He quashes both subpoenas, thereby prohibiting us from reviewing any records and precluding us from submitting records to the grand jury. That grand jury, of course, is comprised of ordinary people. Ladies and gentlemen, no one is above the law. But for the first time, a judge is ruling that a grand jury subpoena—legal in all regards on its face—can be ignored because a judge thinks the subject is beyond reproach. This is a decision that is untethered to the law. It creates chaos where any defendant who wishes to evade an investigation, guilty or not, can allege, "I'm a victim, I'm being targeted, and therefore you cannot investigate me." And if you find the right judge, they will buy it. This is the antithesis of American justice. Exonerating anyone without any records, without an investigation or question, is not how our criminal justice system works. This judge has put himself at the entrance door to the grand jury, slamming that door shut irrespective of the legal process and thus preventing the grand jury from doing the work that it does. So what is the law? According to the United States Supreme Court, the highest court in the land and certainly higher than the court that Boasberg is on, every grand jury has broad discretion. The Court says they may investigate merely on the suspicion that the law is being violated, or even because it wants assurance that it is not. In fact, the Court says a grand jury may act on tips and rumors. Right here are the cases and the citations from the highest court in the land. And yet, this judge is shockingly requiring the government to show something akin to "probable cause"—and those are his words—in order to justify the issuance of a grand jury subpoena. Folks, probable cause is not and never has been the standard that prosecutors in this country need in order to go into a grand jury. This is not and has never been the law of the land. So this decision today by Judge Boasberg runs directly afoul of our highest court's admonition that courts and judges must not and cannot saddle grand juries with mini-trials and preliminary showings. Those things impede a prosecutor's investigation and thus frustrate the public's interest in the fair and expeditious administration of justice. No one, folks, is above the law. This outrageous decision will be appealed by the United States Department of Justice. Go ahead, sir. I'm not here to talk about six members of Congress. I'm here to talk about the fact that the grand jury has a job. They look at evidence and they decide whether they want to indict. If they don't indict, so be it. If you want to know what the judge thinks, go ask him. Next question. You know, honestly, I don't know and I don't care, and I'll tell you why. I am in a legal lane. All of the rest is white noise. I don't care what they say. I have a job and I have the ability to go into a grand jury. There are questions that the American public and people in DC are entitled to know, such as where a billion dollars has gone, and that's my focus. Did you hear what I just said? I just said that this decision will be appealed by the United States Department of Justice. That's the answer to your question. We're looking at 1001 and we're also looking at a statute having to do with fraud, but it's up to the grand jury to make that decision. We're a billion dollars over in cost overruns. Are you kidding? A billion dollars? We're not talking about huge buildings here. Go ahead. I don't even know who he is. Okay, I don't know how to explain this, but I'm going to try. I've been a prosecutor, a judge, a DA, and now a United States Attorney for well over three decades. Politics is not the lane I'm in right now. I have a charge and an oath to the Constitution. My job is to present evidence, and I can do so merely on the suspicion that the law is being violated or even just because I want assurance that it's not. I'm not in those lanes and I will not allow myself or my staff to be in those lanes. That's why we brought crime down in this jurisdiction. We are focused on the law and the people of the district; we are not focused on politics. Go ahead. First of all, he's totally wrong in his assessment of the dates, and that's why we're doing a motion to reconsider, because he got his dates wrong. As far as I'm concerned, this was something that was of public interest. It was something that the Senate Banking Committee wanted information on, and it is something within my jurisdiction and my charge. But I want to tell you a story. When I was a young prosecutor, I was one of the first people to investigate child abuse as a crime. Everyone thought it was a social problem and not a criminal justice problem, thinking the kids are better off with their parents. There was an organization that didn't have a very good reputation, and it was one of the societies that was out there trying to prevent cruelty to children. I set up a relationship where they would bring me cases. People were outraged that I would deal with this organization. My answer to them 30 or 40 years ago was, "I'll deal with the devil. I'll take a case from the devil if you can give me information that will lead me to possibly find a crime." It doesn't matter where a case comes from. It doesn't matter. I can have a suspicion, I can read the newspaper, or I can just want to make sure he didn't commit a crime. So don't make the judge's mistakes; he is wrong on his dates. That's why we're not only appealing, we're making a motion to reconsider. Okay, I want to say one more thing, and I think it's important that you guys hear this from me. There's another way of looking at this, and that is that it's not just the public being fed up with this stuff. It's the fact that we've got grand jurors who are supposed to hear this, and they haven't been able to hear any of it because someone has decided that they're not entitled to hear it. Can you take this? Thanks. This process has been arbitrarily undermined by an activist judge. We have a process. We have been asking for information about a matter that has raised questions in many people's minds—Senate Banking, the public, the papers, it doesn't matter. So give me the information so I can assess it. Give me the information so the grand jury, comprised of regular Americans, can say there's nothing here, and I'll accept it the way I accept other cases. Whatever it is, we'll abide by it. What we don't accept is a judge standing in front of the door of a grand jury blocking our access. That is a role that has never been envisioned. It is a role that will now be used by other defense attorneys around the country who are going to come in with a client and say, "Oh, my client is being targeted, don't you dare go in the grand jury." If a judge says yes, the guy's bathed and washed in immunity and you can't go after him. The process should have been allowed to run its course and it wasn't. Shame on them. Oh, cut it out. Do you know how many convictions we've got? Cut it out. You're in one lane. We have cleaned up this city. Yeah, historic, really? I'll tell you what's historic. What's historic is that I prosecute everything other than 10% of the cases, whereas the United States Attorney before me didn't prosecute 67% of the cases. That's what's historic. I'm willing to take a not guilty or a no true bill because I'll take all the crimes and put them in. Thank you. Thank you, that's all the time we have.

Iran War, Oil Shock, Off Ramps, AI's Revenue Explosion and PR Nightmare

All-In Podcast(1.0M) ·80:23 ·251.6K views · 2026-03-13
Brad Gerstner (Founder, Altimeter Capital) Chamath Palihapitiya (Co-host, All-In Podcast) Jason Calacanis (Co-host, All-In Podcast)
This briefing analyzes the geopolitical and economic volatility of the Iran conflict, the unprecedented revenue scaling of AI leaders Anthropic and OpenAI amid a deepening PR crisis, and the accelerating migration of high-net-worth individuals following new state-level "millionaire taxes.
TL;DR This briefing analyzes the geopolitical and economic volatility of the Iran conflict, the unprecedented revenue scaling of AI leaders Anthropic and OpenAI amid a deepening PR crisis, and the accelerating migration of high-net-worth individuals following new state-level...

TL;DR

This briefing analyzes the geopolitical and economic volatility of the Iran conflict, the unprecedented revenue scaling of AI leaders Anthropic and OpenAI amid a deepening PR crisis, and the accelerating migration of high-net-worth individuals following new state-level "millionaire taxes."

Geopolitical Conflict and Energy Volatility

  • Oil Market Instability: Brent crude experienced massive volatility, spiking to $119/barrel before stabilizing near $100 following attacks on commercial ships in the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Economic Fallout: Goldman Sachs updated its PCE inflation forecast from 2.1% to 2.9% while lowering GDP forecasts by 30 basis points due to energy price shocks.
  • The "Trump Doctrine" vs. Neocons: Brad Gerstner and David Sacks argue the current administration favors a pragmatic approach—degrading threats and declaring victory—rather than the "neocon" pursuit of long-term regime change or nation-building.
  • The China Angle: Chamath Palihapitiya posits that the conflict is ultimately a lever for China. Since China depends on the region for 20% of its oil, President Xi has an enormous incentive to negotiate a "grand bargain" with the U.S. to ensure energy security and avoid domestic unrest (linked to China's 25% youth unemployment).
  • Escalation Risks: Sacks warns that escalating beyond the current "off-ramp" risks Iranian retaliation against Gulf state desalination plants and oil infrastructure, which could render the region uninhabitable and destroy the global economy.

The AI Revenue Explosion: Anthropic vs. OpenAI

  • Unprecedented Growth: Both companies are scaling faster than any software companies in history.
      • Anthropic: Reached a $14B run rate, growing from $1B to $14B in just 14 months. It is currently valued at approximately $380B.
      • OpenAI: Ended 2025 at a $20B annualized run rate, growing 10x in 24 months. It is valued at roughly $840B.
  • The "Experimental Revenue" Debate: Chamath Palihapitiya argues much of this revenue is currently "experimental" board-mandated spending rather than production-grade utility. Brad Gerstner counters that the models have crossed a threshold into "labor augmentation," displacing human labor budgets rather than just IT budgets.
  • Capital Intensity: Building the necessary infrastructure is becoming exponentially more expensive. A one-gigawatt data center now costs upwards of $50B to build, with a 5–6 year payback period.
  • Breakout Use Case: David Sacks identifies coding assistants as the first true "breakout" enterprise use case, as companies can now purchase code on a metered basis rather than being limited by the scarcity of human software engineers.

AI’s Public Relations Nightmare and Infrastructure Friction

  • The Sentiment Gap: AI popularity in the U.S. has plummeted, polling near the Democratic Party and autocratic states. In contrast, 80% of citizens in China view AI as beneficial.
  • Regulatory Capture: Sacks argues that AI CEOs are intentionally using "doomer" narratives to invite regulation, creating a "permissioning scheme" that protects incumbents from smaller competitors.
  • Infrastructure Protest: Local opposition to data centers is rising. In 2025, approximately 25 data center projects (5 gigawatts) were canceled due to protests, representing roughly $50B in lost annual revenue.
  • Open Source Tailwinds: Jason Calacanis notes that startups are increasingly moving toward local, open-source models for 85% of their token usage to avoid high costs and vendor lock-in with frontier labs.

The "Millionaire Tax" and Wealth Migration

  • Washington’s New Tax: Washington state passed a 9.9% tax on income over $1M (effective 2029).
      • High-Profile Exits: Billionaire Starbucks founder Howard Schultz announced his move from Seattle to Miami on the same day the tax passed, following Jeff Bezos’s 2023 departure.
  • California’s Wealth Tax Failure: A Hoover Institution study on California's proposed "billionaire tax" found a 71% chance it would result in a negative Net Present Value (NPV), potentially creating a $25B budget hole as the wealthiest residents flee.
  • The Federal Outlook: Sacks warns of a "socialist" shift in the Democratic platform, pointing to Bernie Sanders and Ro Khanna’s proposals for annual national wealth taxes of up to 5% of total assets.

***

Notable Quotes

"The Trump Doctrine is far more pragmatic than the neocon doctrine. He wants to destroy and degrade threats to America’s national security interests. He doesn’t want to spread democracy." — Brad Gerstner
"There is not a single good example of sustained positive margin expansion and impact of AI inside of a true corporate enterprise that is not right now a small test... Let’s not oversell what this moment is." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"These CEOs are scaring the bejeezus out of the public... they see a strategy there, they’re going for a regulatory capture agenda to create a licensing scheme they can control." — David Sacks
"You either on the side of business and entrepreneurs and creativity... or you’re against it. That’s what’s at stake here." — Brad Gerstner

Alex Karp (CEO, Palantir)

TL;DR Palantir is positioning itself as the "Iron Man suit" for institutional experts, driving a shift from traditional software to AI-integrated operations that CEO Dr. Alex Karp argues will determine the winner of the US-China geopolitical struggle. The "Golden Child" of...

TL;DR

Palantir is positioning itself as the "Iron Man suit" for institutional experts, driving a shift from traditional software to AI-integrated operations that CEO Dr. Alex Karp argues will determine the winner of the US-China geopolitical struggle.

The AI Industrial Revolution and Market Outlook

  • The "Golden Child" of AI: Dan Ives (Wedbush Securities) identifies Palantir as the standout of the "fourth industrial revolution," predicting the company could reach a trillion-dollar market cap within 2–3 years driven by explosive commercial growth.
  • The "Ghost Trade" Phenomenon: Ives describes the current market as a "ghost trade" where legacy software companies are being punished for lack of innovation, while winners like Palantir, Salesforce, and ServiceNow are separating themselves through tangible AI utility.
  • Market Resilience: Despite geopolitical "white-knuckle" moments, Ives argues that selling tech stocks due to global tensions has historically been the wrong move, asserting that tech will hit new all-time highs this year.
  • Physical AI Leaders: NVIDIA and Tesla are identified as the primary plays for "physical AI" (humanoid robots and autonomous vehicles), with NVIDIA owning the platform and Tesla owning the autonomous stack.

The Evolving Role of the Forward Deployed Engineer (FDE)

  • Acceleration to Zero: Ted Mabrey (16-year Palantir veteran) notes that the time to deploy functional applications has dropped from months to a single day, driven by market demand and mature infrastructure.
  • Augmentation vs. Replacement:
      • The "John’s Iron Man Suit" Model: Rather than using AI to replace employees for management's sake, Palantir focuses on augmenting the "factory floor expert" (the person who knows why things break) to make them hyper-productive.
      • The End of "Tortured Explanations": Customers no longer want to understand how the software works; they want to see it work immediately in a "show me" economy.
  • The FDE as CapEx: Palantir treats FDEs as a capital expenditure investment—installing the "factory" of intelligence and training customers to wield it, rather than acting as traditional consultants.

Defense, Autonomy, and Geopolitical Strategy

  • Counter-Drone Infrastructure: Eric Brock (CEO, Ondas) highlights that drones have become a commodity, but the "autonomy layer" and command-and-control systems are where the value lies.
      • He views counter-drone technology as a decade-long infrastructure build-out necessary for protecting both military and commercial targets.
  • US vs. China: Dr. Alex Karp emphasizes that there are only two relevant AI powers: the US and China. He argues that the US must win the AI race for moral and survival reasons, as adversaries like Iran will use lethal AI regardless of US domestic hesitations.
  • Nationalization Risks: Karp predicts a future political movement to nationalize AI technologies if they lead to mass white-collar unemployment and extreme wealth inequality.
  • The "Tribal Knowledge" Advantage: Palantir’s moat is its ability to code the "tribal knowledge" of an institution—its specific regulatory rules, laws, and operational quirks—into a logic that AI can then extend.

Sector Case Study: Western Sports and Data Analytics

  • "Moneyball" for Rodeo: Casey Lane (Teton Ridge) discussed partnering with Palantir to build a video analytics platform for bull riding.
      • Predictive Modeling: The system tracks animal and rider movement to predict outcomes and explain scoring to fans with data-driven graphics.
      • Performance Metrics: Similar to NFL "Next Gen Stats," rodeo is beginning to track bull "bucking power" and rider velocity to professionalize the sport’s broadcast.
  • The Cultural Renaissance: A combination of the "Yellowstone effect" and COVID-19 has driven a surge in interest in the Western lifestyle, allowing athletes to monetize their personal brands through the creator economy.

SIEPR Economic Summit 2026 | Fireside Chat with Mario Draghi, former Prime Minister of Italy

Stanford Institute for Economic Policy Research (SIEPR)(35.1K) ·51:06 ·338 views · 2026-03-13
Mario Draghi (Former Prime Minister of Italy)
Mario Draghi calls for a radical overhaul of European economic policy to bridge a widening "innovation gap" with the U.S.
TL;DR Mario Draghi calls for a radical overhaul of European economic policy to bridge a widening "innovation gap" with the U.S. and China, warning that while global economies have shown remarkable post-pandemic resilience, Europe faces an existential threat from high energy...

TL;DR

Mario Draghi calls for a radical overhaul of European economic policy to bridge a widening "innovation gap" with the U.S. and China, warning that while global economies have shown remarkable post-pandemic resilience, Europe faces an existential threat from high energy costs and stagnant high-tech productivity.

Post-Pandemic Resilience and Emerging Fiscal "Cracks"

  • Unprecedented Adaptability: Draghi noted that the global economy outperformed expectations following COVID-19 and the energy crisis due to massive policy backstops and a private sector that diversified supply chains faster than anticipated.
  • Fiscal Sustainability Concerns: Despite recent resilience, Draghi identified "staggering" deficits as the primary risk to future stability.
      • Inflationary Pressures: He argued that nearly every policy move in the last four years—excluding deregulation—has been potentially inflationary.
      • The Growth Solution: Markets have not yet priced in high debt levels because of the "AI and technological revolution," which offers a path to deficit sustainability through massive productivity gains.
  • The "Luck" Factor in Europe: Draghi cautioned Europe against complacency, noting that recent growth was buoyed by "coincidences," including Germany’s defense spending and frontloaded exports due to U.S. tariffs.

Monetary Policy and the "Whatever It Takes" Legacy

  • The Power of Credibility: Reflecting on his 2012 pledge to save the Euro, Draghi emphasized that the statement's success relied on two pillars: it was strictly within the ECB's mandate (arguing price stability is impossible if the currency evaporates) and the intervention was "unlimited."
  • Critique of European Austerity: Draghi argued that Europe’s "fiscal austerity" after 2010 was a strategic error.
      • U.S. vs. Europe: The U.S. injected three to seven times more public money into its economy than Europe did following the Great Financial Crisis, allowing the U.S. to return to pre-crisis investment levels in 18 months, whereas Europe took nine years.
  • Defending Quantitative Easing (QE): Counterfactual studies suggest that without QE, Europe would have seen 1% lower growth and 1% higher unemployment. He attributed the "late" tightening of interest rates to a failure to realize that Russia was deliberately manipulating natural gas supplies years before the invasion of Ukraine.

The Draghi Report: Solving Europe’s Competitiveness Crisis

  • The Innovation Gap: Draghi clarified that European "laziness" or welfare states are not the cause of the productivity lag. When excluding the high-tech sector, European and U.S. productivity growth are identical; the gap exists solely because Europe failed to lead in disruptive tech.
  • Capital Misallocation: A critical barrier is the funding structure of research and development (R&D).
      • U.S. Model: 80% of R&D is privately funded.
      • European Model: 80% of R&D is publicly funded, failing to leverage private capital.
  • Investment Needs: Draghi’s report estimates Europe needs €800 billion to €1.2 trillion in annual investment to remain competitive, particularly in defense and energy. He advocates for a "European DARPA" to lead disruptive innovation.

Geopolitics and the End of Complacency

  • Energy as Industrial Policy: Europe faces a unique disadvantage with energy prices that hamper global competitiveness. Draghi argued that industrial policy must now be a "combination of energy, innovation, and trade policy."
  • Shift in Self-Awareness: Draghi is "cautiously optimistic" because the "immense complacency" of European CEOs and governments has finally shattered. Leaders now recognize they are at the "depth of the abyss," which is the necessary precursor for radical reform.
  • A Slow Exit from the Station: He compared the European Union to a train finally moving out of a station; while the progress is currently slow, he expects it to gain speed as defense and innovation reforms are implemented.
"If your currency evaporates, there is no more price stability to keep." — Mario Draghi
"The US economy, US government injected in the economy here... between three and seven times [the] public money... injected in Europe. And that explains a lot of the differences in growth, productivity and everything else." — Mario Draghi
"Everything that is in place now points to go down, not up, take any metric you may want to choose. And that's the sort of awareness I had been fighting for. Now that's there and now people understand that." — Mario Draghi

The organization was originally established as the Richard M. Lucas Foundation for Prostatic Cancer Research, created during the final months of Richard's life. The foundation was formed to honor his memory and to help advance research. Uncle Richard was 52 years old. We also expanded to Alzheimer's research and other causes generated by John and Don Lucas. My father joined SIEPR; he joined the SIEPR advisory board in 1988. He served as chair of the advisory board from 2001 to 2007 and helped launch the first SIEPR Economic Summit in 2004. Mario Draghi is one of the most influential economic policymakers of our era, an Italian economist, central banker, and public servant. He was president of the European Central Bank from 2011 to 2019, where he played a pivotal role in navigating the eurozone through the sovereign debt crisis. He subsequently served as Prime Minister of Italy from 2021 to 2022. He also recently authored the influential Draghi Report on the future of Europe's competitiveness for the European Commission. No. No, because energy prices at that point went down, so they supported real wages, personal consumption expenditure and the economy stood up basically. And then we have on the private sector side, we didn't expect private sector companies and firms to be so capable of diversifying quickly from and without major disruption, disruptions in the supply chains. That is something that's rarely observed, but actually it was a fundamental component because we had observed disruption in supply chains during COVID, so we did expect that that could last. Not as high as here, but basically most European countries don't have fiscal space. Now this was in the context of defending the euro, an existential threat to the euro. I'm curious about the confidence of "and believe me, it will be enough." Where does that confidence come from? Would it be harder, is it going to be harder to have that confidence today if there's some crack that emerges that requires us to address it? Well as you may imagine, I was asked this question many times in the last 13 years. So that was quite useful for me to put together and remember how exactly things went, which is the answer to the question: why such a line had such a success? Now the first thing is context. That was probably arguably the lowest point for the European Central Bank. We had raised interest rates twice in 2011 in the midst of the worst recession we ever had since the Second World War. Then at the end of 2011, I start my tenure as President of the ECB and we start lowering interest rates. Notice, nobody was actually asking for it in Germany. So that came as a shock, first surprise. Then we took a series of unprecedented decisions on monetary policy like extending long-term maturity, long-term credit lines for banks. Markets were not impressed, not impressed. And Europe continued spiraling down. Then in June we had a momentous decision actually by all the leaders of Europe; they created the banking union with one banking supervisor. Markets had not reacted. So that's when it happens that I give this line. So that was the context. Then you have communication. Yes, those words had never been stated before in Europe in that way and that was part of the success. But the other part was substance. What I say is not only "believe me, it's going to be enough" or adequate, but also "and it's within my mandate." Actually, I was speaking about the ECB, so it's within its mandate. And that was important because it made it credible because the first reaction was oh, he's overstepping the mandate of the ECB. Because the mandate is pretty narrow; it says we should look at price stability. So how come I didn't overstep this line in saying we're doing whatever it takes to... well the answer there is that the euro was about to disappear, to evaporate. And so if your currency evaporates, there is no more price stability to keep basically. I think that that was the... I mean for me now we look at that with certain distance, but at that time was something I really felt very much under pressure because of this. But the other thing was that all this didn't come as something that was an improvisation. There had been meetings before; there were papers, secret papers of course, studying this. So that statement came in the way it came because of communication, but the substance of the statement had been vastly prepared before by the staff of the ECB. And then of course, within a few days' time, the governing council confirmed that and issued a press statement where this monetary policy stance would be confirmed for the years to come. And the difference, and that's the last part, perhaps it's the most important part of the reason of success, was that the interventions that we would make would be unlimited. Because you see, there had been before programs of purchasing government bonds of different countries, but they were limited and markets easily saw through them and basically destroyed the program. So that program was meant to be unlimited and that's why. So I gave you a pretty sort of structured answer because I had 13 years to think about that. To practice, to practice the answer and put things together, really. I'm probably going to ask a follow-on question here for which you have had a lot of practice as well, so I apologize to you. But if I take you back to an earlier crisis, one that impacted you a little bit differently than it did us here in the United States, the global financial crisis. You were governor of the Bank of Italy and then you were elected the first chair of the Financial Stability Board of central bankers, bank supervisors, and I think you were elected that by your peers if, as I recall. You know, with friends like that you don't need enemies, so... There are people, there are a lot of people that think that the problems we have today, socially, economically, whatever, trace back to some of the stepping beyond the mandate, the traditional behavior, that occurred during the global financial crisis, specifically quantitative easing. And so I want to ask you about that from your perspective at the time, governor of Bank of Italy, but you also undertook quantitative easing at the ECB. Would there have been an alternative? Would you say, thinking about it, "I wouldn't do that again," especially now as we're thinking about quantitative tightening if you will, or at least no more QE? Or do you think that that is a tool that appropriately stays in people's toolbox? No, I think you ask two questions, because in the case of Europe, QE came several years, four years actually after the Great Financial Crisis. But they are right in saying that many developments—social developments, economic, political developments—go back to the Great Financial Crisis and the responses that were given to it. By the way, incidentally as chairman of the Financial Stability Board, I remember, and this was early, it was 2007 when we start seeing the first cracks in the system with the first subprime crisis. And the G20 and the G7, the G20 of President Bush and the G7 with Secretary Paulson, asked the Financial Stability Board and myself to prepare a report. And there are people here who remember that. And so we came out four-five months later, in April 2008, with a series of recommendations—many, 77, for all kinds of sectors of the financial sector, all kinds of businesses in the financial sector. And I should say most of them, if not all of them, have been adopted, were later adopted by Congress. In Europe instead, various people gold-plated upon them and added regulation upon regulation and basically killed a part of the financial sector industries. For example, securitization was killed in Europe; it has been dead for something like more than 10 years and it goes back to that decision without any reason to do so in Europe, by the way. Because securitization in Europe was actually performing pretty well; the default rates were close to zero. So it was an entirely different context. But basically the response that was given to the Great Financial Crisis in terms of fiscal policy is probably one of the reasons why we are experiencing so much populism in Europe since then, both left-side populist and right-extreme. And the reason is that the fiscal policy that was adopted after 2010 has been totally wrong. Basically what happens is that after 2010, we enter into a period called fiscal austerity. So all European countries started tightening budgets, all their budgets and so on without interruption. To give you an idea: investment in Europe, private sector investments, go back to the level pre-Great Financial Crisis only in 2019. In this country, they were back to the level after one year and a half after the Great Financial Crisis. If you take the years after, depending on which year, depending on the time span, you see that the US government injected in the economy here in this country about seven times the public money injected in Europe—between three and seven times. And that explains a lot of the differences in growth, productivity, and everything else. So this I think was mostly the fiscal response. In terms of monetary policy, I mentioned before the mistakes that we made in 2011, which compounded on a wrong fiscal policy. And so the QE time comes after, and it comes after in... I think it was 2014 that I announce in August, actually here in Jackson Hole, the undertaking of QE in Europe. Well, and people asked whether this had consequences or not. Well, given that fiscal policy was actually hampering growth all throughout, the monetary policy response accounts for the creation of something between three and four million jobs. And when I finished with my tenure at the ECB, it was November '19, so I take responsibility for everything that happened until then, not later. But there was a study there, a counterfactual study, showing that if we didn't have QE—only QE, so I'm not speaking about interest rates—we would have had an inflation rate lower by 1% and growth lower by 1% and unemployment higher by more than 1%. So that's the post-mortem of QE. Then what happened after that, whether the prolongation of QE too long after that was a source of inflation in Europe, I don't know. Point is—and that's probably what I was about to say before—in Europe too, tightening came late. But the reason why it came late in Europe is that everybody kept on saying that the increase in the price of energy was temporary. And the reason why it was thought that it was temporary is that in fact it was the outcome, now speaking specifically of the natural gas prices, that was the outcome—deliberate outcome—of President Putin's strategy starting in 2020. The strategy was to make sure that the Russian company delivering natural gas to Europe would deliver at the contractual minima so that by the time he started the war in Ukraine two years later, all European countries would have their stocks at the lowest possible level. And so prices had started going up two years in advance really, and nobody knew why. So that is the justification that I would give for a delayed monetary tightening in Europe. Right, life is difficult to predict sometimes, huh? Very difficult. And we now have another natural gas situation going on now, also impacting Europe. I'll come back to that. I want to be sure we have some time to talk about more current events, specifically this remarkable... I've been calling it a paper; it's not a paper, it's a book. This is "The Future of European Competitiveness." You can get it bound on Amazon or you can download it and print it off—be sure you have lots of paper in your printer if you do that. And there are, I already know, a number of questions about that and stepping towards where we are today in terms of Europe. Before we go there, as a way of segueing, I just want to ask you a personal question. You did your PhD at MIT under two absolute giants in economics: Franco Modigliani and Robert Solow. And then you taught for a few years, I believe primarily in Italy, but you pretty quickly moved into work with the Bank of Italy and the Italian Ministry of the Treasury and the World Bank. I was curious: did you always know that your destiny was going to be in civil service and public policy? Oh no, absolutely no. No? No. No, actually it was not that quick because I spent about a total of about 15 years being an academic. But let me say just one thing. That time at MIT was sort of a miracle because we not only had Franco Modigliani and Bob Solow, but I also had the privilege of having Peter Diamond in theory, I had Bob Engle in econometrics, and I had been a TA for Paul Samuelson. But basically no, then after those years as a professor, then I kind of was curious to know more about policy. And after that, I was taken up so much by policy, by debt, by developing countries—that was the time of the debt crisis in Latin America. So at that time, then at the end I became head of the Treasury in Italy, so I went back. And I probably had to go through, in the first five years, the most critical time in my country when Italy was really on the verge of defaulting. It was a country that was ranked Triple A in its debt and lost, I think in two years' time, it was around Single B or something like that. So it was really... and at that time you realize that what you do actually matters and you can make a difference. The issues that you raised in it are ones that are in the news now all the time. Yeah, well first let me say one thing about how this report came out because it tells also why the substance was so sort of so up-to-date, so well received. And then the third leg was the incredible contributions by many academics—two Nobel prizes, maybe three—that simply had volunteered in getting interested in the issue. And they are, in a sense, also responsible for the narrative of the report, which is the part that is eminently readable. The book is about detailed prescriptions sector by sector. Scott Bessent: Inside Trump’s Treasury; War Costs; & Why Bond Market is King

The Master Investor Podcast with Wilfred Frost(40.0K) ·53:09 ·59.3K views · 2026-03-13
Scott Bessent (US Treasury Secretary) Wilfred Frost (Host, The Master Investor Podcast)
US Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent asserts that the bond market remains the ultimate arbiter of global finance, while outlining a US strategy of aggressive energy releases and military escorting in the Strait of Hormuz to stabilize markets amidst the ongoing Iranian conflict.
TL;DR US Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent asserts that the bond market remains the ultimate arbiter of global finance, while outlining a US strategy of aggressive energy releases and military escorting in the Strait of Hormuz to stabilize markets amidst the ongoing Iranian...

TL;DR

US Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent asserts that the bond market remains the ultimate arbiter of global finance, while outlining a US strategy of aggressive energy releases and military escorting in the Strait of Hormuz to stabilize markets amidst the ongoing Iranian conflict.

Investment Philosophy and the Primacy of the Bond Market

  • Market Skepticism: Bessent attributes his success to a "healthy skepticism" of elite institutions and consensus opinion, noting that the biggest opportunities arise when the crowd is wrong (e.g., the 2000s housing crisis or Japan’s "lost decades").
  • The "King" of Markets: The US Treasury market is viewed as the most critical global indicator. Bessent emphasizes that his primary concern is continuous functioning and price discovery, rather than daily price fluctuations.
  • Macro Strategy: Successful global macro investing requires the ability to "imagine a different state of the world" and correctly identify when the market has mispriced that potential outcome.
  • Risk Management: Bessent’s core advice for investors is to avoid getting "out over your skis" to ensure they are never forced to sell at the bottom or chase at the top.

The Iranian Conflict and Global Energy Security

  • Strait of Hormuz: Bessent confirms that as soon as it is militarily feasible, the US Navy—potentially with an international coalition—will begin escorting oil tankers through the straits.
  • Oil Price Mitigation:
      • SPR Release: The US and IEA have authorized a 400-million-barrel release from strategic reserves, the largest in history, to counter price spikes.
      • Price Outlook: While WTI has peaked near $115, Bessent believes the *duration* of the spike is more critical to the US economy than the level itself. He predicts a move to a "substantially lower oil price regime" once the conflict is resolved.
  • War Costs: The conflict has cost approximately $11 billion thus far. Bessent maintains that the US has sufficient financial cushions and will not "pause" operations due to budget concerns.
  • Status of the Regime: Bessent suggests the Iranian leadership is severely degraded, questioning the status and "compos mentis" of the Ayatollah following US military strikes.

Trade Policy and International Relations

  • Tariff Reconstruction: Following a Supreme Court ruling limiting emergency powers, the administration is moving global 10% tariffs to "Section 301" and "Section 122" authorities. Bessent views this as a restoration of previous policy rather than a new escalation.
  • Friction with the UK: Bessent acknowledges "bumps in the road" with Prime Minister Keir Starmer, specifically regarding delayed access to the Diego Garcia base, which forced US B2 bombers into 37-hour round trips and increased risk to American lives.
  • Russia and India: A 30-day waiver was granted to Indian refiners for Russian oil to maintain global supply, though Bessent expressed "unfortunate" regret that Russia might briefly benefit from the current market volatility.
  • Western Hemisphere: Bessent sees a "generational opportunity" as Latin American countries (e.g., Argentina, Chile) shift from far-left to center-right, market-friendly governments.

The Federal Reserve and Domestic Economy

  • Federal Reserve Reform: Bessent prefers the Bank of England’s model of Quantitative Easing (QE), arguing that extraordinary measures should only be used in extraordinary times, rather than the Fed's multi-year buying cycles.
  • Economic Resilience: Despite high debt-to-GDP levels, Bessent argues the US "chip stack" is stronger than ever due to:
      • Energy Dominance: Transitioning from a net importer to a major exporter.
      • AI Leadership: The US is projected to control 70–80% of global compute power.
      • Growth Projections: Anticipating 3% GDP growth post-conflict, significantly outperforming European peers.
  • Shadow Banking: The Treasury is monitoring "wobbles" in private credit, though Bessent clarifies that the government has no obligation to protect investors in the shadow system from the downside of their risks.

Notable Quotes

"Unlike most of my predecessors, I have a very healthy skepticism of elite institutions and elite opinion... but I have a healthy regard for the market." — Scott Bessent
"One thing you learn as a lifeguard: drowning people will try to pull you down... sometimes you have to give them a little nudge to be able to save them." — Scott Bessent
"The Japanese yen trade... I have no idea [if the policies will work], but it will be the market ride of a lifetime." — Scott Bessent (recalling a conversation with George Soros)
"In terms of investing... you just shouldn't get out over your skis. You never want to have to sell at the bottom; you never want to find yourself chasing at the top." — Scott Bessent

Dylan Patel — The Single Biggest Bottleneck to Scaling AI Compute

Dwarkesh Patel(1.2M) ·150:45 ·77.7K views · 2026-03-13
Dylan Patel (Founder, SemiAnalysis) Dwarkesh Patel (Host, Dwarkesh Patel Podcast)
The primary bottleneck for scaling AI compute is shifting from short-term constraints like power and data center construction to the long lead-time semiconductor supply chain, specifically EUV lithography tools and High Bandwidth Memory (HBM).
TL;DR The primary bottleneck for scaling AI compute is shifting from short-term constraints like power and data center construction to the long lead-time semiconductor supply chain, specifically EUV lithography tools and High Bandwidth Memory (HBM). Rapid scaling is currently...

TL;DR

The primary bottleneck for scaling AI compute is shifting from short-term constraints like power and data center construction to the long lead-time semiconductor supply chain, specifically EUV lithography tools and High Bandwidth Memory (HBM). Rapid scaling is currently driving a massive "memory crunch" that will significantly increase the cost and decrease the performance of consumer electronics as resources are diverted to AI.

The Economics of Hyperscale CapEx and Lab Scaling

  • Forward-Looking Spend: A significant portion of the $600 billion combined CapEx from the "Big Four" (Amazon, Meta, Google, Microsoft) is being spent on "setup CapEx" for 2027–2029.
      • Long Lead Items: This includes down payments on turbines, power purchasing agreements, and land acquisition rather than just current-year chips.
  • The Inference Revenue Trap: AI labs like Anthropic and OpenAI are entering a phase where revenue growth requires massive incremental compute.
      • Gigawatt Scaling: Anthropic likely needs 4 gigawatts of inference capacity just to sustain its current revenue growth trajectory, separate from its R&D and training needs.
  • OpenAI vs. Anthropic Strategy: OpenAI has been more aggressive in securing long-term (5-year) compute deals with neo-clouds like Coreweave and Oracle.
      • Flex Capacity: Anthropic’s more conservative approach has left them potentially paying higher spot-market margins or relying on revenue-share agreements with Google and Amazon to bridge the compute gap.

Logic and the ASML Bottleneck

  • ASML as the Ultimate Linchpin: While power and data centers are bottlenecks today, by 2028–2029, the bottleneck falls to the production of EUV (Extreme Ultraviolet) lithography tools.
      • Tool Math: It takes roughly 3.5 EUV tools to manufacture 1 gigawatt’s worth of NVIDIA’s "Rubin" chips.
      • Supply Constraints: ASML produces only ~70-80 tools per year, with plans to reach ~100 by the end of the decade. This physical limit creates a hard ceiling on global AI chip production.
  • Underinvestment in Fabs: There is a mismatch between data center construction (which takes ~8 months) and semiconductor fab construction (which takes 2-3 years).
      • The AGI Disconnect: Dylan Patel argues that the broader semiconductor supply chain is not yet "AGI-pilled"—manufacturers do not fully believe in the trillion-dollar demand projections, leading to persistent under-capacity.
  • The Performance Gap: Moving back to older process nodes (like 7nm) to bypass tool shortages is inefficient because the performance gulf between generations is compound.
      • Beyond Flops: The 20x performance difference between chip generations often comes from networking, interconnects, and memory bandwidth rather than just raw transistor counts.

The Memory Crunch and Consumer Impact

  • The HBM Penalty: High Bandwidth Memory (HBM) is essential for AI but requires 3 to 4 times the wafer area of standard DRAM to produce the same amount of memory.
      • Demand Destruction: To supply the AI industry, memory vendors must "destroy" consumer demand. This is leading to a halving of production for low-to-mid-range smartphones and PCs.
  • Consumer Cost Spikes: Memory price increases could add $150–$250 to the Bill of Materials (BOM) for high-end devices like the iPhone.
      • The "Hate AI" Factor: Patel predicts consumer resentment toward AI will grow as PCs and smartphones stop getting better and instead become more expensive and less capable due to diverted components.
  • Fab Lag: No meaningful new memory fabs will come online until late 2027 or 2028, ensuring the memory crunch remains a persistent drag on scaling.

Geopolitics: US vs. China Scaling

  • Fast vs. Slow Takeoff: Patel posits a "Fast timelines = US wins; Long timelines = China wins" framework.
      • US Advantage: Currently, the US and its allies control the leading-edge logic supply chain (3nm/2nm). If AI capabilities advance rapidly, the US can leverage this hardware lead into an insurmountable software/intelligence lead.
      • China’s Strategic Scale: China is building a fully vertical, indigenous supply chain. While they lack EUV today, they may achieve massive scale with DUV (Deep Ultraviolet) and 7nm multi-patterning by 2030, potentially out-scaling the West if the intelligence race plateaus or takes longer.
  • Distillation Barriers: As US labs move toward agentic, multi-step "reasoning" models, it becomes harder for Chinese competitors to "distill" or copy American model outputs, further widening the gap.

By the way, so there's this interesting economics effect called Alchian-Allen, which is the idea that if you increase the fixed cost of different goods, one of which is higher quality and one of which is lower quality, that will make people choose the higher quality good on the margin. So to give you a specific example, suppose the better-tasting apple costs $2 and then like the shittier apple costs $1. Okay, now suppose you put an import tariff on them. And so now it's $3 versus $2 for like good apple, medium apple, right? Is that because they both increased by a dollar or should it be like 50% increase? No, because they both increased by a dollar, the whole effect is that if there's a fixed cost applied to both, the relative price difference between them, the ratio changes. So previously it was like the more expensive one was 2x more expensive and now it's just 1.5x more expensive. So I wonder if applied to AI, that would mean that look, if GPUs are going to get more expensive, there will be a fixed cost increase in the price of compute. As a result, that will push people to be willing to pay higher margins for slightly better models because the calculus is I'm going to be paying all this money for the compute anyway, I might as well just pay slightly more to making sure it's like the very best model rather than a model that's slightly worse. You're at roughly 2 million EUV passes for a single gigawatt. It's scanning, stepping across. And it does this hundreds of times across the entire—or dozens of times across the whole wafer. And so when you're talking about, hey, how many EUV passes, that's the entire wafer being exposed at a certain rate. You'll do some EUV, you'll do a bunch of different process steps depositing stuff, etching stuff, cleaning the wafer, dozens of those steps before you do another EUV layer. There's a spec called overlay, right, which is okay you did all this work, you drew these lines on the wafer, now I want to draw these dots, right? Let's just say I want to draw these dots to connect this—these lines of metal to then holes and then the next layer up is another set of lines goes perpendicular, so now you're connecting wires going perpendicular to each other. You have to land them on top of each other, so it's called overlay. And overlay is a spec that's been improved rapidly by ASML. Wafer throughput has been improved rapidly by ASML. I think there are a couple factors here. ASML has not decided to just go "YOLO, let's expand capacity as fast as possible." In general, the semiconductor supply chain has not. It's lived through the booms and busts, and we can talk a bit more about it, but basically no one—some players as of very recently have woken up—but in general, no one really sees demand for 200 gigawatts a year of AI chips or trillions of dollars of spend a year in the semiconductor supply chain. Yeah, I hear you, but I'm just saying like no one really understands this in the supply chain. Constantly we're told our numbers are way too high, and then when they're right they're like, "Oh yeah yeah, but your next year's numbers are still too high." And it's like—but anyway, ASML has sort of—their tool has four major components. It has the source, which is made by Cymer in San Diego. It has the reticle stage, which is made in Wilmington, Connecticut. It has the wafer stage and the optics, the lenses and such. Those two are made in Europe. And so when you look at each of these four, they're tremendously complex supply chains that, A, they have not tried to expand massively, and B, when they try to expand them, the time lag is quite long. And so again, this is the most complicated machine that humans make, period, at any sort of volume. But let's talk about the source specifically. What does the source do? It drops these tin droplets and it hits it three subsequent times with the laser perfectly. So the first one hits this tin droplet, expands it out, hits it again so it expands out to this perfect shape, and then it blasts it at super high power. The tin droplets get excited enough that they release EUV light at 13.5 nanometer, and then it's in this thing that is basically collecting all the light and directing it into the lens stack. Then you have the lens stack which is Carl Zeiss, as you mentioned, and some other folks, but Zeiss is the most important part of it. They also have not tried to expand production capacity because they don't see any—they're like, "Oh yeah yeah, like we're growing a lot because of AI, we're growing from 60 to 100." It's like, no no no, we need to go to like a couple hundred, but it's fine, whatever. Each of these tools has, I think, 18 of these lenses effectively, or mirrors. They are multilayer mirrors which are perfect layers of molybdenum and ruthenium, if I recall correctly, stacked on top of each other in many layers, and then the light bounces off of it perfectly. But it's not just like when we think about a lens in a shape that focuses light. This is like a mirror that is also a lens, and so it's quite complicated. Any defect in this perfect layer of stack—in these super thinly deposited stacks—will mess it up. Any curvature issues—there are a lot of challenges with scaling the production. It's quite artisanal in this sense because you're not making tens of thousands of these a year; you're making hundreds or thousands. You talk about 60 tools a year, 18 of these per tool, you end up with—you're still in the hundreds of tools, or you're at the thousand number roughly for these lenses and projection optics. So then you step forward to the reticle stage, which is also something really crazy. This thing moves at, I want to say, 9Gs—like it will shift 9Gs because as you step across a wafer, the tool will go—and the wafer stage is complementary, it's the wafer part. So you line these two things up, you're taking all the light through the lenses that's focused, and here's the reticle, here's the wafer, and you're passing—the reticle's moving one direction, the wafer is moving the other direction as it scans a 26 by 33 millimeter section of the wafer. And then it stops, it shifts over to another part of the wafer and does it again. And it does that in just seconds, and each of them are moving at 9Gs in opposite directions as it's doing this. So each of these is like a wonder and marvel of chemistry, fabrication, mechanical engineering, and optical engineering because you have to align all these things and make sure they're perfect. All these things have crazy amounts of metrology because you have to perfectly test everything. If anything is messed up, the yield goes to zero because this is such a finely tuned system. And by the way, it's so large that you're building it in the factory in Eindhoven, Netherlands, and they're deconstructing it and shipping it on many planes to the customer's site. Then you're reassembling it there and testing it again, and that process takes many, many months. So there's so many steps in the supply chain, whether it be Zeiss making their lenses and projection optics, or Cymer, which is an ASML-owned company making the EUV source. Each of these has its own complex supply chain. ASML's commented that their supply chain has over 10,000 people in it—like individual suppliers. Yes, and it might not be directly; it might be through like, "Hey, Zeiss has so many suppliers and XYZ company has so many suppliers." But you know, if you just think about like, okay, you're talking about two physically moving objects that are this large and this large, the size of a wafer, and it has to be accurate to the level of single-digit nanometers or even smaller because the entire system, the layer-to-layer variation, has to be on the order of 3nm. And so if the overlay is 3nm, that means each individual part, the accuracy of its physical movement, has to be even less than that. It has to be sub-1nm in most cases because the error of these things stacks up. And so there's no way to just snap your fingers and increase production. Things simple as power—the US going from 0% power growth to 2% power growth, even though China's already at 30%, was so hard for America to do. And that's a really simple supply chain with very few people in it who make difficult things. There's probably what, 100,000 electricians or people working in the supply chain of electricity in the US. And you know, when you look at ASML, they employ so few people. Carl Zeiss probably employs less than a thousand people working on this. And all of those people are like super, super specialized. So you can't just train random people up for this in the snap of a finger. You can't just get your entire supply chain to galvanize. NVIDIA's had to do a lot to get the entire supply chain to even deliver the capacity they're going to make this year. Right, I think we're in fast takeoff-ish. It's not like we're talking about a Dyson sphere by X date, it's more like the revenue is compounding at such a rate that it does affect the economic growth. Okay, let's go back to memory because I think this is maybe people on Wall Street and people in the industry are understanding how big this is, but maybe generally people don't understand how big a deal this is. So we've got this memory crunch as you're talking about, and earlier I was asking about oh could we solve for the EUV tool shortage by going back to 7nm? So let me ask a similar question about memory. HBM is made of DRAM but has three to four times less bits per wafer area than the DRAM it's made out of. Is it possible that accelerators in the future could just use commodity DRAM and not HBM? And so we could make much more capacity out of the DRAM we get. And the reason I think this might be possible is look, if we're going to have agents that are just going off and doing work and it doesn't really—you don't—it's not a synchronous chatbot application, then you don't necessarily need extremely high fast latency kinds of things anymore. And so maybe you can have the low bandwidth because the reason you stack DRAM into stacks and make HBM is for higher bandwidth. And so is it possible to go to HBM-less accelerators and basically have the opposite of Claude-Fast, like have Claude-Slow? Yeah, I think at the end of the day the incremental purchaser who is willing to pay the highest price for tokens also ends up being the one that's less price sensitive. The compute should be allocated in a capitalistic society towards the goods that have the highest value. And the private market determines this by willingness to pay. And so to some extent, sure, Anthropic could actually release a slow mode. They could release Claude Slow Mode and have an increase in tokens per dollar by a significant amount. They could probably reduce the price of Opus 4.6 by 4x or 5x and reduce the speed by maybe just 2x. Like, the curve on inference throughput versus speed is there already just on HBM. And yet they don't, because no one actually wants to use a slow model. And furthermore on these agentic tasks, you know, it's great that the model can run at this time horizon of hours, but if the model was just running slower, that hours would become a day, or vice versa; if the model was running faster, that hours becomes an hour. And yet no one really wants to move to that day-long wait period because the highest value tasks also have some time sensitivity to them. And so I struggle to see, you know, yes you could use DDR, but then there's a couple of things that are challenging with this. You could use regular DRAM. One is you're still limited—you know, one of the core constraints of chips is like a certain size, all of the I/O escapes on the edges of the chip. So oftentimes what you see is the left and the right of the chip are HBM, the I/O from the chip to the HBM is on the sides, and then the top and bottom are I/O to other chips. And so if you were to change from HBM to DDR, then all of a sudden this I/O on this edge would have significantly less bandwidth, but it has significantly more capacity per chip. And so the metric that you actually care about is bandwidth per wafer, not bits per wafer. Because the thing that is constraining the flops is just getting in and out the next matrix, and for that you just need more bandwidth. Yeah, getting out the weights and getting in and out the KV cache. Right. And so in many cases these GPUs are not running at full memory capacity. Yes, it's obviously a system design thing—model, hardware, software co-design of how much KV cache do I do, how much do I keep on the chip, how much do I offload to other chips and call when I need it for tool calling or whatever, how many chips do I parallelize this on? Obviously the search space of this is very broad, which is why we have things like Inference-X, which is an open source model that searches all the optimal points on inference for a variety of eight different chips and models. Anyway, the point is you don't necessarily—you're not always necessarily constrained by memory capacity. You can be constrained by flops, you can be constrained by network bandwidth, you can be constrained by memory bandwidth, or you can be constrained by memory capacity. There's sort of like four, if you're really to simplify it down—there's like four constraints, and each of these can break out into more. But in this case, if you switch to DDR, yes, you produce 4x the bits per DRAM wafer, but all of a sudden the constraints shift a lot and your system design shifts a lot. You go slower, yes. Is the market smaller? Okay, maybe possibly. But also now all of a sudden all these flops are wasted because they're just sitting there waiting for memory. It's like great, I don't need all that capacity because I can't really increase batch size because then the KV cache is going to take even longer to read and so you can't— Yeah. Interesting. What is the bandwidth difference between HBM and normal DRAM? Yeah, so an HBM stack of HBM4—let's just talk about the stuff that's in Rubin because that's what we're indexed on—is 2048 bits across connected in an area that's like 13 millimeters wide roughly, or 11. And in that shoreline you have 2048 bits transferring at 10 giga-transfers per second. You multiply those together and you divide by 8, bits to bytes, you're at roughly 2.5 terabytes a second per HBM stack. When you look at DDR, in that same area it's maybe 64 or 128 bits wide and that DDR5 is transferring at anywhere from 6.4 giga-transfers a second to maybe 8,000 giga-transfers a second, so your bandwidth is significantly lower. It's 64 times 8,000 divided by 8, you're at 64 gigabytes a second. And even if you take a generous interpretation of 128 times 8 giga-transfers, you're at 128 gigabytes a second for the same shoreline versus 2.5 terabytes a second. There's an order of magnitude difference in bandwidth per edge area. And if your chip is a square, or it's 26 by 33, which is the maximum size for a chip individual die, you only have so much edge area and then on the inside of that chip you put all your compute. There's things you can do to try and change—more SRAM, more caching, blah blah blah—but at the end of the day you're very constrained by bandwidth. Interesting. But at a deeper level, interruptions can throw off a model's understanding and degrade the quality of its responses. And it's not always clear why. Labelbox realized that this was a huge bottleneck for their customers. So they built an evaluation pipeline called EchoChain to help you diagnose and fix your voice model's specific failure modes. EchoChain starts by feeding conversations into your voice model. It then injects interruptions at specific intervals and classifies any failures into one of three different modes. One: did it acknowledge the correction but keep the old plan? Two: did it adapt briefly but then slide back to old assumptions? Or three: did it abandon the old task entirely? This is extremely useful information because Labelbox can get your model the exact data it needs to fix whatever issue is preventing it from being a viable and competent voice model. The way you can sort of compare them and think about them is—sure, it's one thing to shrink process technology and make the transistor smaller and each chip has X number of flops. But you forget the big gating factor which is these models don't run on a single chip, they run on hundreds of chips at a time. If you look at DeepSeek's production deployment which is well over a year old now, they were running on 160 GPUs. And that's what they serve production traffic on. And so they split the model across 160 GPUs. Every time you cross the barrier of a chip to another chip, there is an efficiency loss because you now have to transfer over a high-speed electrical SerDes and there is a latency cost, there's a power cost, there's all these dynamics that hurt. As you shrink and shrink and shrink the process node, you've increased the amount of compute in a single chip. Now in-chip movement of data is at hundreds of—at least tens of terabytes a second if not hundreds of terabytes a second. Whereas between chips you're on the order of a terabyte a second. And so this movement of data between chips that are super close to each other physically—and you can only put so many chips close to each other physically. So you have to put chips in different racks. The order of data between that is on the order of hundreds of gigabits a second—400 gig, 800 gig a second. So 100 gigabytes a second roughly. And so you've got this huge ladder of: on chip I can communicate at super-fast speeds; within the rack I can communicate at order-magnitude speeds; outside the rack I can communicate at an even order-magnitude lower than that. And as you break the bounds of chips you end up with this performance loss. Anyway, the reason I explain this is because when you look at Hopper versus Blackwell, even if both of them are using a rack worth of chips, the Hopper is significantly slower because the amount of performance that you have leveraged to the task within that domain is much, much higher. The tens of terabytes a second of communication between these transistors or these processing elements, and terabytes a second between these processing elements, means the performance is much higher. Yeah, so earlier I was mentioning how communication within a chip is super fast; communication within chips that are in the same rack is fast but it is not as fast—it's on the order of terabytes. And then communication very far away is on the order of gigabytes, hundreds of gigabytes. So this order of magnitude as you get further distance. Compute, maybe across the country, it's on the order of gigabytes per second. Scale-up domain is this tight domain where the chips are communicating on the order of terabytes a second. And so for NVIDIA, previously this meant an H100 server had eight GPUs and those eight GPUs could talk to each other at terabytes a second. With Blackwell, NVL72, they implemented rack-scale scale-up, and that meant all 72 GPUs in the rack could connect to each other at terabytes a second speed. And the speed doubled gen-on-gen, but also the most important innovation they did was going from 8 to 72 in the domain. When we look at Google, their scale-up domain is completely different, right? It has always been on the order of thousands. With TPU v4 they had pods the size of 4,000 chips; with v8 they have pods even at, or v7 they have pods in the 7,000 or—sorry—8,000, 9,000 range. And what's relevant here is that it's not just the same as NVIDIA; it's not like-for-like. Google has a topology that's a torus, right? So every chip connects to six neighbors. Rather than NVIDIA, the 72 GPUs in the rack connect all-to-all, right? So they can send terabytes a second to each other to any arbitrary other chip in that pod of scale-up. Whereas with Google, you have to bounce through chips, right? So this means if TPU 1 needs to talk to TPU 76, then it has to bounce through various chips. There is always some blocking of resources when you do that, because that one TPU is only connected to six other TPUs. And so there's a difference in topology and bandwidth, and there are tradeoffs and advantages of both, right? Google gets to have a massive scale-up domain, but then they have the tradeoff of having to bounce across chips to get from one chip to another; you can only talk to six direct neighbors. And so there is like this tradeoff, and Amazon has mutated their scale-up domain. They're somewhere in between NVIDIA and Google effectively where they're trying to make larger scale-up domains. They try and do all-to-all to some extent, which is what with switches—which is what NVIDIA does—but also to some extent they use torus topologies like Google does. And as we advance forward to next generations, all three of them are moving more and more towards a dragonfly topology. This means there's sort of like there is some fully connected elements and there are some elements that are not fully connected, so you can get the scale-up to be hundreds or thousands of chips, but also have it not contend for resources when you're bouncing through chips. So does that explain why parameter scaling has been slow? I think it's partially the capacity and bandwidth, but also as you build a larger model, the ability to deploy it is slower, right? In terms of, like, hey, what is the inference speed for the end user? That's kind of irrelevant. What's really relevant is RL. And what we've seen with these models and allocation of compute at a lab is sort of there's a few main ways you can allocate compute. You can allocate it to inference, i.e. revenue; you can allocate it to development, i.e. making the next model; and you can allocate it to research. And in development specifically, you split it between pre-training and RL. And so when you think about, hey, what exactly is happening? Well, the compute efficiency gains you get from research are so large you actually want most of your compute to go to research, not to development, because you know all these researchers are generating new ideas, trying them out, testing them, and continuing to push the Pareto optimal curve of scaling laws further and further and further. And at least what we've seen empirically is, like, model cost gets 10x cheaper every year, or even more than that. Which, at the same scale, gets 10x cheaper, or to reach new frontiers it costs the same amount or more, right? So you don't want to allocate too many resources to pre-training and RL; you actually want to allocate most of your resources to research. And then in the middle is this sort of like development period. If you pre-train a 5-trillion-parameter model, now you have to spend all this time—how many rollouts do you have to do in these RLs? And these rollouts for a trillion-parameter model versus a 5-trillion-parameter model are five times larger, which then means it takes more time. If you wanted to do as many rollouts, maybe the larger model is more sample-efficient. Let's say it's 2x more sample-efficient. Okay, great. Now you need 2.5x much time of RL to get the model smarter. Or you could RL the smaller model for 2x the time and you'd still have a 25% difference in the big model which is 2x more sample-efficient and doing X number of rollouts versus the small model which is a trillion parameters. Although it's less sample-efficient, it's doing twice as many rollouts, so it still does it faster. And so you get the model faster, sooner, and you've done more RL, and then you can take that model to help you build the next model to help your engineers train and do all these research ideas. And so this feedback loop is actually weighed towards smaller models in every case, no matter what your hardware is. And then as you look to Google, Google does deploy the largest production model of any of the major labs, right? With Gemini Pro, it is a larger model than GPT-4, it's a larger model than Opus. And so you end up with, yes, Google does this because they have a unipolar set of compute, right? All almost all TPU. Whereas Anthropic is dealing with H100s, H200s, Blackwell, Trainium, TPUs of various generations, right? And OpenAI is dealing with mostly NVIDIA right now but going towards having AMD and Trainium as well. The fleets of compute like Google can just optimize around a larger model and they can leverage a thousand chips in a scale-up domain to get, you know, the RL speed much faster so that you can actually have this feedback loop be fast. But at the end of the day, in isolation, you almost always want to go with a smaller model that gets RL'd faster and gets deployed into research and development, so you can build the next thing and get more compute efficiency wins. And then this compounding effect of—oh, I made a smaller model that I RL'd more that I then deployed into research and development earlier, and I spent less compute on the training itself because I was able to allocate more compute to the research. This like compounding effect of being able to do the research faster and faster and faster is potentially a faster takeoff. And that's all these companies want is the fastest takeoff possible.
