Anyway, so there's this interesting economics effect called Alchian-Allen, which is the idea that if you increase the fixed cost of different goods, one of which is higher quality and one of which is lower quality, that will make people choose the higher quality good on the margin. So to give you a specific example, suppose the better-tasting apple costs $2 and then like the shittier apple costs $1. Okay, now suppose you put an import tariff on them. And so now it's $3 versus $2 for like the good apple and the medium apple, right? Is that because they both increased by a dollar or should it be like a 50% increase? No, because they both increased by a dollar, the whole effect is that if there's a fixed cost applied to both, the relative price difference between them, the ratio, changes. Previously it was like the more expensive one was 2x more expensive and now it's just 1.5x more expensive. So I wonder if applied to AI, that would mean that look, if GPUs are going to get more expensive, there will be a fixed cost increase in the price of compute. As the result, that will push people to be willing to pay higher margins for slightly better models. The calculus is: I'm going to be paying all this money for the compute anyways, I might as well just pay slightly more to making sure it's like the very best model rather than a model that's slightly worse. Right, so the Hopper went from $2 to $3, and if the Hopper can make a million tokens of Opus and it can make 2 million tokens of Sonnet, the price differential between Opus and Sonnet has decreased because the price of the GPU has increased by a dollar from 2 to 3. And so across these three different buckets, each of these requires different amounts of EUV, right? So when you manufacture a wafer, there's thousands and thousands of process steps where you're depositing material, removing them. But the sort of key critical step, which at least in advanced logic is like 30% of the cost of the chip, is something that doesn't actually put anything on the wafer, right? You take the wafer, you deposit photoresist—which is like a chemical that basically chemically changes when you expose it to light—and then you stick it into the EUV tool, which shines light at it in a certain way. It patterns it, right, because there is what's called a mask, which is a stencil effectively for the design. And so when you look at a wafer, you know, leading-edge 3nm wafer has 70 or so masks, right? 70 or so layers of lithography, but 20 of them are the most advanced EUV, right? And that specifically, you know, if you think about okay, well if I need 55,000 wafers for a gigawatt, if I do 20 EUV passes per wafer, you then—you can do the math, that's like 1.1 million passes of EUV for a single gigawatt. So actually, like, it's pretty simple. And then once you add the rest of the stuff, it ends up being 2 million, right? Across 5nm and all the memory. You're at roughly 2 million EUV passes for a single gigawatt. You know, these tools are very complicated, so when you think about what it's doing across a wafer, it's taking the wafer and it's scanning and then it's stepping across, right? It's scanning, stepping across. And it does this hundreds of times across the entire—or dozens of times across the whole wafer. And so when you're talking about, hey, how many EUV passes, that's the entire wafer is being exposed at a certain rate. An EUV tool can do roughly 75 wafers per hour, and the tool is up roughly 90% of the time, right? So in the end, you end up with: actually I need about three and a half EUV tools to do the 2 million EUV wafer passes for the gigawatt. So three and a half EUV tools satisfies a gigawatt. Often it’s easy to boil things down to flops per watt or flops per dollar, but that’s actually not a fair comparison. This is where you can look at Kimi K2.5 or DeepSeek. When you look at these two models and you look at their performance on Hopper versus Blackwell on very optimized software, you get vastly different performance. Most of this is not attributed to flops; a lot of this is numerics. Because those models are actually 8-bit, it's not like Blackwell and Hopper are both optimized for 8-bit and Blackwell’s not really taking advantage of its 4-bit there. The performance gulf is actually much larger. The way you can sort of compare them and think about them is that it’s one thing to shrink process technology and make the transistors smaller so each chip has X number of flops. But you forget the big gating factor, which is that these models don’t run on a single chip. They run on hundreds of chips at a time. If you look at DeepSeek’s production deployment, which is well over a year old now, they were running on 160 GPUs. That’s what they serve production traffic on. They split the model across 160 GPUs. Every time you cross the barrier of a chip to another chip, there is an efficiency loss because you now have to transfer over a high-speed electrical SerDes. There is a latency cost, a power cost, and all these dynamics that hurt. As you shrink the process node, you’ve increased the amount of compute in a single chip. Now, in-chip, movement of data is at least tens of terabytes a second, if not hundreds. Whereas between chips, you’re on the order of a terabyte a second. This movement of data between chips that are physically super close can only be pushed so far. So you have to put chips in different racks. The order of data between that is on the order of hundreds of gigabits a second—400 gig, 800 gig a second, so roughly 100 gigabytes a second. You’ve got this huge ladder: on chip, I can communicate at super-fast speeds; within the rack, I can communicate at an order-of-magnitude lower speed; outside the rack, I can communicate at an even lower order of magnitude. As you break the bounds of chips, you end up with this performance loss. Anyway, the reason I explain this is because when you look at Hopper versus Blackwell, even if both are using a rack worth of chips, the Hopper is significantly slower. The amount of performance you have leveraged to the task within each domain—tens of terabytes a second of communication between transistors versus terabytes a second between elements—is much higher, and therefore the performance is much higher. When you look at inference at, let's say, a hundred tokens a second for DeepSeek and Kimi K2.5, the performance difference between Hopper and Blackwell is on the order of 20x. It's not two or three times like the flops performance difference indicates, even though those are on the same process node. There are just differences in networking technology and what they’ve worked on. You can translate some of these back. But when you look at Rubin on 3nm, some of these things are just not possible to do back on A100, even if you make a new chip for 7nm. There are certain architectural improvements you can port, and there are certain ones you cannot. The performance difference is not just going to be the difference in flops; it’s cumulative between flops per chip, networking speed between chips, and memory bandwidth on a single chip and an entire system. All these things compound. Can I ask a very naive question? The performance difference is still going to be much larger than the flops difference you mentioned. Often it’s easy to boil things down to flops per watt or flops per dollar, but that’s actually not a fair comparison. This is where you can look at Kimi K2.5 or DeepSeek. When you look at these two models and you look at their performance on Hopper versus Blackwell on very optimized software, you get vastly different performance. Most of this is not attributed to flops; a lot of this is numerics. Because those models are actually 8-bit, it's not like Blackwell and Hopper are both optimized for 8-bit and Blackwell’s not really taking advantage of its 4-bit there. The performance gulf is actually much larger. The way you can sort of compare them and think about them is that it’s one thing to shrink process technology and make the transistors smaller so each chip has X number of flops. But you forget the big gating factor, which is that these models don’t run on a single chip. They run on hundreds of chips at a time. If you look at DeepSeek’s production deployment, which is well over a year old now, they were running on 160 GPUs. That’s what they serve production traffic on. They split the model across 160 GPUs. Every time you cross the barrier of a chip to another chip, there is an efficiency loss because you now have to transfer over a high-speed electrical SerDes. There is a latency cost, a power cost, and all these dynamics that hurt. As you shrink the process node, you’ve increased the amount of compute in a single chip. Now, in-chip, movement of data is at least tens of terabytes a second, if not hundreds. Whereas between chips, you’re on the order of a terabyte a second. This movement of data between chips that are physically super close can only be pushed so far. So you have to put chips in different racks. The order of data between that is on the order of hundreds of gigabits a second—400 gig, 800 gig a second, so roughly 100 gigabytes a second. You’ve got this huge ladder: on chip, I can communicate at super-fast speeds; within the rack, I can communicate at an order-of-magnitude lower speed; outside the rack, I can communicate at an even lower order of magnitude. As you break the bounds of chips, you end up with this performance loss. Anyway, the reason I explain this is because when you look at Hopper versus Blackwell, even if both are using a rack worth of chips, the Hopper is significantly slower. The amount of performance you have leveraged to the task within each domain—tens of terabytes a second of communication between transistors versus terabytes a second between elements—is much higher, and therefore the performance is much higher. When you look at inference at, let's say, a hundred tokens a second for DeepSeek and Kimi K2.5, the performance difference between Hopper and Blackwell is on the order of 20x. It's not two or three times like the flops performance difference indicates, even though those are on the same process node. There are just differences in networking technology and what they’ve worked on. You can translate some of these back. But when you look at Rubin on 3nm, some of these things are just not possible to do back on A100, even if you make a new chip for 7nm. There are certain architectural improvements you can port, and there are certain ones you cannot. The performance difference is not just going to be the difference in flops; it’s cumulative between flops per chip, networking speed between chips, and memory bandwidth on a single chip and an entire system. All these things compound. Can I ask a very naive question? This year and last year, the B200 now has two dies on a single chip, so you can get that bandwidth on a single chip without having to go through NVLink or InfiniBand. Next year, Rubin Ultra will have four dies on one chip. What is preventing us from just doing that with an old node? How many dies could you jam on a single chip and still get these tens of terabytes a second? Even within Blackwell, there are differences in performance when you're communicating on the chip versus across the chips. Those bounds are obviously much smaller than when you're going out of the entire chip, but they exist between each die versus within the package. When you scale the number of chips up, there is some performance loss. It's not just perfect, but it is way better than different entire packages. Now, how large can advanced packaging scale? The way NVIDIA is doing it is CoWoS, as are Google with Broadcom and Marvell, and Amazon with Trainium. But you can also look back at what Tesla did with Dojo. To date, it is still probably the best chip for running convolutional neural networks. Anyway, Dojo chips were optimized around that. They made a big package, but at the same time, as you make packages bigger, you have other constraints like networking speed, memory bandwidth, and cooling capabilities. It’s not simple. But at the end of the day, anything you do on 7nm you can also probably do on 3nm in terms of packaging. Real conversations are full of fits and starts and pauses and interruptions. I mean, just listen to this episode. At least superficially, voice models have gotten pretty good at handling these kinds of things. But at a deeper level, interruptions can throw off a model's understanding and degrade the quality of its responses. And it's not always clear why. Labelbox realized that this was a huge bottleneck for their customers. So they built an evaluation pipeline called EchoChain to help you diagnose and fix your voice model's specific failure modes. EchoChain starts by feeding conversations into your voice model. It then injects interruptions at specific intervals and classifies any failures into one of three different modes. One: did it acknowledge the correction but keep the old plan? Two: did it adapt briefly but then slide back to old assumptions? Or three: did it abandon the old task entirely? This is extremely useful information because Labelbox can get your model the exact data it needs to fix whatever issue is preventing it from being a viable and competent voice model. So if you want to ensure that your voice model stays performant in real conversations, you should reach out to Labelbox. Go to labelbox.com/dwarkesh. And most of this is not attributed to flops; a lot of this is and numerics, right? Because those models are actually 8-bit. So it's not like Blackwell and Hopper—they're both optimized for 8-bit and Blackwell's not really taking advantage of its 4-bit there. You know, the performance gulf is actually much larger. And you know, the way you can sort of compare them and think about them is sure, it's one thing to, you know, shrink process technology and make the transistor smaller and each chip has X number of flops. But you forget the big gating factor which is these models don't run in a single chip; they run on hundreds of chips at a time, right? If you look at DeepSeek's production deployment, which is well over a year old now, they were running on 160 GPUs, right? And that's what they serve production traffic on. And so they split the model across 160 GPUs. Every time you cross the barrier of a chip to another chip, there is an efficiency loss because you now have to transfer over a high-speed electrical SerDes and there is a latency cost, there's a power cost, there's all these dynamics that hurt. And so they split the model across 160 GPUs. Every time you cross the barrier of a chip to another chip, there is an efficiency loss because you now have to transfer over a high-speed electrical SerDes and there is a latency cost, there's a power cost, there's a—there's all these dynamics that hurt. As you shrink and shrink and shrink the process node, you've increased the amount of compute in a single chip. Now in-chip, right, movement of data is, you know, at at hundreds of—at least tens of terabytes a second if not hundreds of terabytes a second. Whereas between chips you're on the order of a terabytes a second, right? And and so this movement of data between chips that are super close to each other physically—and then you can only put so many chips close to each other physically. So you have to put chips in different racks. The order of data between that is on the order of hundreds of gigabits a second, right? 400 gig, 800 gig a second. So a 100 gigabytes a second roughly. And so you've got this like huge ladder of like, oh, on chip I can communicate at super-fast speeds, within the rack I can communicate at, you know, order-magnitude speeds, outside the rack I can communicate at an even order-magnitude lower than that. And as you break the bounds of chips you end up with this performance loss. So anyway, the reason I explain this is because when I look at—when you look at Hopper versus Blackwell, even if both of them are using, you know, a rack worth of chips, the Hopper is significantly slower because the amount of performance that you have leveraged to the task within that, you know, within each domain of hey, tens of terabytes a second of communication between these transistors or these processing elements, and terabytes a second between these processing elements is much, much higher and therefore the performance is much higher. So when you look at inference at let's say a hundred tokens a second for DeepSeek and Kimi K2.5, Hopper versus Blackwell, the performance difference is on the order of 20x. Interesting. Not two or threex like the flops performance difference indicates, even though those are on the same process node. You know, there's just differences in networking technology and what they've worked on. And so you can translate some of these back. But when you look at like Rubin, what they're doing on 3nm, some of these things are just not possible to do all the way back on A100 even if you make a new chip for 7nm. There's just like certain architectural improvements you can port, there's certain ones you cannot. And so the performance difference is not just going to be the difference in flops, it's in some senses cumulative. It is cumulative between the difference in, you know, flops per chip, networking speed between chips, how many flops are on a chip versus a system, memory bandwidth on a single chip and on an entire system. JUST IN: VP JD Vance Delivers Remarks In Rocky Mount, North Carolina, Amid Iran War

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·25:32 ·4.1K views · 2026-03-13
JD Vance (Vice President)
Vice President JD Vance visited Rocky Mount, North Carolina, to highlight the Trump administration's economic successes—including significant tax cuts and lowered inflation—while contrasting their "America First" policies against the records of Democ...
TL;DR Vice President JD Vance visited Rocky Mount, North Carolina, to highlight the Trump administration's economic successes—including significant tax cuts and lowered inflation—while contrasting their "America First" policies against the records of Democratic opponents...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance visited Rocky Mount, North Carolina, to highlight the Trump administration's economic successes—including significant tax cuts and lowered inflation—while contrasting their "America First" policies against the records of Democratic opponents regarding immigration and public safety.

Economic Revitalization and Tax Relief

  • The Working Families Tax Cut: Vance highlighted a 1,100-page bill he described as "chock-full of good stuff" for North Carolinians, focusing on three major pillars:
      • Social Security: Implemented the largest tax cut for Social Security benefits in U.S. history.
      • Middle-Income Relief: Established the largest tax cut ever recorded for individuals earning under $100,000 annually.
      • Household Wealth: Claimed that while the average family lost $3,200 in take-home pay under Biden, they have gained $1,300 in wealth during the first year of the current Trump administration.
  • Housing and Interest Rates:
      • Market Trends: Reported that new home purchases are at a five-year high and rents have dropped for six consecutive months.
      • Tax Refunds: Noted that the average tax refund for North Carolina families is approximately $3,700.
  • Small Business Oversight: SBA Administrator Kelly Loeffler has reportedly identified hundreds of millions of dollars in fraudulent payments, with the goal of reaching billions in cuts by year-end.

Contrast with Democratic Leadership

  • Opposition to Rep. Don Davis: Vance heavily criticized incumbent Representative Don Davis, alleging he votes "every single day" with Nancy Pelosi.
      • Legislative Record: Davis reportedly voted against cutting taxes on Social Security, tax cuts for those making under $100k, and measures to increase reporting on violent crime.
  • The Save America Act: Vance emphasized the need for this legislation to prevent non-citizens from voting.
      • Non-Citizen Voting: Cited a case where an illegal immigrant from Mauritania allegedly voted in seven federal elections since 2008.
      • Bipartisan Support: Claimed the act is an "85% issue" supported by a majority of Americans, yet opposed by Davis.
  • Criticism of Gov. Roy Cooper: Accused Governor Cooper of prioritizing foreign interests (Ukraine) over domestic safety, specifically citing a case where a Ukrainian girl, Irina, was killed by a person Vance claimed should not have been on U.S. streets.

National Security and Public Safety

  • Crime Reduction: Vance claimed homicides in the U.S. are at their lowest level in 125 years.
      • Policy Drivers: Attributed this to increased federal support for police and the deportation of "illegal alien criminals."
      • Reporting Standards: Mandated that all localities report crime accurately to better target violent offenders.
  • Iran and Nuclear Weapons: Confirmed the administration is currently engaged in military operations to ensure Iran never acquires a nuclear weapon.
  • Military Support: Acknowledged North Carolina's heavy military presence and called for prayers for service members currently in harm's way overseas.

Supporting North Carolina Industry

  • Furniture Manufacturing: Relayed a specific message from President Trump regarding the protection of the state's craftsmanship.
      • Tax Incentives: Promised tax cuts for companies building furniture facilities within North Carolina.
      • Tariffs: Threatened a "big fat 50% tariff" on foreign companies attempting to undercut local jobs by bringing in foreign-made furniture.
  • Energy Policy: Reaffirmed the administration’s commitment to ending the "war on coal," specifically promoting the use of "clean beautiful coal."

***

How to Win Maximally | Ted Mabrey at AIPCon 9

Palantir(144.0K) ·14:38 ·2.1K views · 2026-03-13
Ted Mabrey (Global Head of Commercial, Palantir)
Palantir is witnessing a dramatic "step change" in deployment velocity where complex enterprise applications are now operationalized in hours or days rather than years, shifting the technology's purpose from incremental efficiency to total market dominance.
TL;DR Palantir is witnessing a dramatic "step change" in deployment velocity where complex enterprise applications are now operationalized in hours or days rather than years, shifting the technology's purpose from incremental efficiency to total market dominance. The pace of...

TL;DR

Palantir is witnessing a dramatic "step change" in deployment velocity where complex enterprise applications are now operationalized in hours or days rather than years, shifting the technology's purpose from incremental efficiency to total market dominance.

Radical Acceleration in Deployment Velocity

The pace of deploying Palantir’s Artificial Intelligence Platform (AIP) has accelerated to a point that transcends traditional software timelines, moving from "vibe-coded" toys to mission-critical infrastructure.

  • Rapid Prototyping: A battery pack manufacturer developed four distinct applications—ranging from technician assistants to corporate quality workflows—within a single 24-hour window following a factory tour.
  • Real-Time Crisis Management: During a breakout of cartel violence in Mexico, a global logistics provider built a global disruption manager in one afternoon to re-route materials and manage quoting live.
  • Fleet Optimization: A new customer seeking better fleet availability built four high-performing models in the first 24 hours; by day four, they had an automated routing engine re-allocating tens of thousands of assets.
  • Legacy Migration: Modernization projects previously estimated at two years are being compressed into weeks.
      • An automotive financing company migrated its finance backbone in two weeks.
      • A major retailer onboarded 2,000 stores to a new system with 99.4% accuracy in two weeks.

From Incremental Efficiency to "Winning Maximally"

Mabrey argues that organizations must reject the "private equity buyout mentality"—using technology only for minor margin bumps through headcount reduction—and instead use it to dominate their sectors.

  • The "Ironman Suit" for Operators: Software is now immediate and adaptable enough that it makes economic sense for a company's most valuable subject matter experts (SMEs) to define the code.
      • This empowers the "substantive operators" who know how the plant runs to scale their intuition 1,000x.
  • Agentic Advocates: Rather than one-off fixes, companies are deploying autonomous agents to act as "advocates" for every SKU, component, or store address.
      • Example: A tier-one automotive supplier uses agents to perform continuous "value engineering" across their entire portfolio, reacting instantly to changes in supplier costs or regulations.
  • Re-Integrating the Enterprise: Instead of managing complexity by isolating teams into silos, winning companies are using the technology to organize "X-Men" style cross-functional teams around specific problems.

Strategic Scaling and Re-Platforming

The speed of scaling is shortening multi-year strategic roadmaps into months, allowing companies to "transform in place."

  • Accelerated Re-Platforming: A software provider facing a five-year proprietary stack migration is now on track to finish in less than one year by using AIP to translate millions of lines of code and regulatory documents into a technical ontology.
  • R&D Modernization: A medical device customer collapsed the "iterative handoff" process between R&D, regulatory, and manufacturing into a CI/CD-style pipeline for new product development.
  • Industrial Throughput: A submarine component manufacturer automated their quoting and engineering workflows, allowing them to move from two shifts to three shifts and increase government order volume.

The New Engagement Model: The 30-Day Sprint

Palantir is evolving its partnership model to match this new velocity, moving toward an aggressive "social contract" with customers.

  • Day 1 to Week 1: Define what "winning" looks like and build the initial solution. Expand "fractally" to capture the upstream and downstream value chain within the first seven days.
  • The 30-Day Intensity Period: Replicate the initial success across every critical value chain in the company during a high-pressure month of deployment.
  • The One-Year Goal: Execute a 12-month plan to integrate the system across the entire business, aiming for a total transformation of the organization’s financials by the end of the term.
"What our best customers are doing is they are holding this technology accountable to enabling them to win. In Palantir terms, to dominate... This is not some technical requirement; it’s ships at sea and subs in the water." — Ted Mabrey
"It certainly never made sense for [top operators] to engage with systems that were designed to rise a tide or drive standardization when they are the thing that is not standard... You can now build Ironman suits around those people so that they can do a thousand X what they could do before." — Ted Mabrey
"We’ll meet you where you are if you aren’t there yet... but I want to be very clear: everything that we’re earning as we build your trust is to get into an operating model that is this aggressive, because this technology is begging to be deployed this aggressively." — Ted Mabrey
As Dr. Karp mentioned in his opening remarks, we hold these conferences so that you all can learn from each other and inspire each other. And we tend to have a policy of allowing no Palantir person on stage. For any of you who have tried to get an extra ticket for one of your colleagues and tried to wrestle with Sasha around this, Sasha enforces that quite militantly. But I thought it was extremely important, and it was worth having that fight to get on stage today, because I'm observing a dramatic step change in what you all are doing with our products. I think that has really material knock-on effects for how you organize around deploying this technology such that you win. And so I wanted to take the time to take a step back and share what we are learning together with you all that I think is going to position us to win together this year and into the future. I'm going to hit three concrete things very quickly. First, what I think has changed. Two, why I think that is so important when we think about how we organize together. And then three, how we're evolving our engagement model to enable you to use this technology to win in the maximalist way. The first thing that has changed, that I think almost everything else I'm going to hit sits downstream of, is the pace of deployment is accelerating really dramatically. I've done nothing but deploy Palantir for 16 years. Literally all I've done. And as I observe what our deployment teams are doing with you all, it looks honestly like a little bit like an alien process to me. Over the last really three months, three to six months, the pace, the scale, and the breadth of what you all are doing with our technology looks like something that I do not recognize. It made me start to wonder: how do we need to adapt and make sure that, given this change, you're able to evolve and use this technology in anger? As examples of that, we had a team that did a factory tour last week at a battery pack manufacturer. We love to do the actual concrete work to see how the thing actually works. They recorded the factory tour, understanding how the factory actually worked and having a discursive discussion about what works well, what breaks, what happens when the product leaves the factory, and the types of constraints that are imposed on the factory that make their job difficult. They then took that recording, fed it into AIP, and integrated it with the technology and the tradecraft that is being coded in AIP. The team, over a 24-hour window, was able to develop four applications: a customer-facing application, an operator-facing application to manage the line, a technician assistant to be able to increase uptime, and a corporate quality workflow across plants. These are addressing upstream issues around design, engineering, and procurement quality. They were able to deliver those four applications in a 24-hour window. Second, we had another deployment that was on-site with a global logistics provider. While on-site, cartel violence broke out in Mexico. This completely disrupted their operation, and they had to re-route a lot of different things. Through the course of the afternoon, the team built a global disruption manager to track what was happening in Mexico, but also extended it for all of the different geographies that they operate in. They then critically took that and linked it into what they observed. What do you actually do when one of these disruptions happen? How do I manage product re-routing, material holds, and changes to the quoting process in terms of influencing what's actually hitting the logistics network? They were able to do that in an afternoon to now have something that is available for the next disruption, like a disruption in the Strait of Hormuz. At a brand new customer where we were just starting with a fleet management challenge, the initial statement was: "Can you build a better predictive model to manage fleet availability?" In the first 24 hours, the team was able to build four models that outperformed the existing state of the art. In the next 24 hours, they built agents that were able to consistently monitor all of the inputs that were describing the actual state of the network and challenge assumptions that the models were built on in order to fix those forecasts live. The next day they integrated that with inventory data, sales forecasting, and an optimization engine. By the fourth day, they had an automated routing engine that was suggesting ways to re-allocate tens of thousands of assets for maximum throughput and quality. And that happened in four days. Lest you think that this type of pacing is only happening in clean, pristine new environments, you already heard from SAP and Accenture today about how this technology is allowing you to accelerate the rate at which you can get to the technology you want to run by migrating away from the old technology that you have. We're seeing that across legacy systems everywhere. There was an automotive financing company that was intending to migrate their finance backbone, projected to be a two-year plus project, that was able to execute that in two weeks. A major global retailer that was trying to onboard 2,000 stores onto a new system, expecting that to be a two-year problem, was able to do that to 99.4% accuracy for 200 stores in two weeks, only limited by the data availability and the presumption that you could do all this this quickly. All of that pacing is really changing the perception of what you should be demanding from software. If you want something and you want it to be valuable, it should be ready immediately. These applications and the things that are happening quickly are not vibe-coded peripheral applications that are sort of toys on the periphery of your business. The speed of scaling is also dramatically accelerating. As another example, we have a customer that is a software provider with a legacy software stack built on old proprietary technology, but it serves a mission-critical function for its customers. They're undergoing a re-platforming effort that they expected to take five years from a planning perspective, with of course attendant risk in actually executing that plan. By working together, taking a codebase that was millions of lines of code and requirements documents across regulatory requirements and user journeys in hundreds of thousands of documents, and translating that into a granular technical ontology—every code file, every potential column that is going to be added to the database—they were able to build the ontology to not only track and understand the interdependencies across that project, but also have a foundation to run long-running agents to start to suggest and build new products so that they could not only re-platform but transform in place. That project that was expected to take five plus years is now expected to be done in less than a year. Of course, our implementation team is accountable to continuing to accelerate that every single day. With a medical device customer, they started with, "Hey, can we use AI in order to proactively monitor our adherence to the regulatory and compliance requirements that we have in a heavily regulated industry for our manufacturing and process controls?" Through the course of building that application very quickly, it became clear that there was another issue, which is just understanding the full opportunity base of existing products in thousands of different SKUs that have been accumulated over time through acquisition in different MES systems, PLMs, and ERPs. How do I actually understand the current base of what I have and the feature set in order to serve my aggregate customers? And then another R&D team that is constantly surveying the market landscape, reading literature about innovations, studying what competitors are releasing, and understanding evolutions in standard of care that create market opportunities and focus points for their R&D. How can I then collapse all of those things into something where I have the equivalent of CI/CD for new product development? Instead of iterative handoffs of a new idea that doesn't meet regulatory controls and months and months of handoffs, they are dramatically moving that process to something where, as they can innovate, they can hit all of the compliance checks that are required to push new products out. For a drone manufacturer, the first problem initially in the first day was, "Can we just get a better handle on what is happening with our in-service fleet? The availability, where they're having mechanical issues, where they have service issues." It became very clear that it is valuable to get a handle on that within the first 24 hours, but it really wasn't particularly valuable if you couldn't integrate that back with the existing operation of what you do about it. How can I automate purchase orders? How can I re-plan production? How can I make sure that my supply chain is coordinated with that? How do I automate customer interaction so that I can actually get a technician out there, oftentimes in austere environments? If you're not solving all of those problems together at pace, you're not actually doing something; you're just pushing the squishy balloon and moving efficiency around. The last thing that is really dramatically changing is by doing the work, we're discovering the work that is actually valuable to do. Instead of the targeted insertion of incremental efficiencies, we have a tier-one automotive supplier that does value engineering to re-imagine and re-engineer every single one of their components in every single one of their parts. They think about: Can I use a cheaper material? Can I move from shrink-wrap around a wire to a plastic covering? Re-engineering each one of those components is a very lucrative exercise for them to do, but it is also very time-consuming and costly. It's cross-functional, and there's a lot of controls associated with it. By doing it once, you then are able to create a foundation where you can create an agent that is essentially operating as an advocate for every single component in every single product on every single customer every single day, understanding every change in the dependent variables—supplier costs, new regulations, new requirements from the end customer—to do that value engineering at scale across the entirety of the portfolio. Similarly, and perhaps in a way that only a Palantirian could see as similar, that same dynamic is playing out with a fashion retailer that needs to understand: do I have the right product with the right material in the right place at the right time? But when you realize that's not just a product management perspective, because the market is not just the market—each store is different, each sizing is different. Can I have that coordinated, essentially agentic advocate for each address in the same way that I do for each monitor or each wiring harness? We're seeing this with a medical provider that is able to automate and understand their revenue cycle management. The automation of that is valuable but incremental, but the real value is giving them more confidence to understand what new clients they can take on to increase their practice and increase the utilization of their actual providers. We're seeing this with a manufacturer deep in the industrial base that builds hatch covers for submarines. By automating the quoting workflow and getting engineers back on the floor, they could create more certainty in terms of what they could provide, which means the government could order more parts. This means they were able to move from two shifts to three shifts and do more of what they do in order to win, not just do the same thing more efficiently. So taking a step back, why that all matters, why I think it matters for you, and the thing that I think is changing in terms of a paradigm shift with how you think about deploying and integrating with this technology: what our best customers are doing is they are holding this technology accountable to enabling them to win. In Palantir terms, to dominate. And I think what I mean by that perhaps is best expressed by saying what it's not. What they are doing is essentially rejecting what I would pejoratively label the private equity buyout mentality of how to deploy and use technology. "I want to do the same thing a little bit more efficiently with a software provider from the software industrial complex that provides the lowest common denominator solution of what I do elsewhere so I can do the exact same thing with fewer heads and then have maybe a short-term margin bump until the rest of the market adapts." This is not interesting, and it is not the highest and best use of this technology. It needs to be, and because of the pacing and the way at which it can operate, technology is in the fight for these customers. Whether that is literally in the fight supporting something like Operation Epic Fury or as you heard from Admiral Ocana, what it is accountable to as ShipOS is not some technical requirement; it's to ships at sea and subs in the water. I loved that line, and thinking about what is "ships at sea and subs in the water" for your business in terms of what you're trying to accomplish. One of the reasons that I think the technology and that pacing allows it to be operating in the fight is that, for the first time, it actually makes economic sense for your most valuable substantive operators to be engaged in defining what the software is that runs your business. Because the software is now immediate, adaptable, and to the extent it is embodied as our Silicon Valley brethren would have you believe, it is yearning to unlock itself to do what you want it to do. When it can be immediate and you can have that turnaround, it becomes the highest and best use of your best people. Everybody knows these people, whether they're in the CEO role or they're at the edge—that person who the place doesn't run without. They just want to do the work; maybe they don't want to be the manager. It certainly never made sense for them to engage with systems that were designed to rise a tide or drive standardization when they are the thing that is not standard. They have the intuition, they know how the plant runs, they know how to underwrite the policy, whatever it might be. You can now build Ironman suits around those people so that they can do a thousand times what they could do before and set the standard for what is unique about your organization that you want to do. Or that can come from the CEO. The most motivating customer says: "This is what I want my company to be. This is what winning in my industry is going to look like in 2030, I understand it." And then it's my job in order to manifest the initial MVP of an implementation of that company by Easter. The final thing that we're seeing our customers do is that they're using the technology to re-integrate their companies. Instead of having the sequential conceptual or literal manufacturing process of managing complexity by isolating teams that are served by specific isolated systems with specific boundaries, they're organizing around the actual problem that they want to solve. You now get actual quick and empirical returns from having those people cross-functionally in the same room operating together like the X-Men. Finally, I'm going to wrap up with how we're evolving our engagement model and how we would like to engage with you. I like to think of this almost as like the social contract that we want to have. The first thing that we need from you is we need you to tell us what your business is going to look like if it wins. That can come from the CEO of course, but it can also come from the edge; it can come from that substantive operator where we can build together and then build momentum to give them the thing that they know actually is required to make things work for your company. What you're going to see happen is you're going to expect that the minute you start working on that, you're going to create an initial solution. Then you're going to realize that the actual value of the solution isn't going to be captured unless I can start to integrate workflows upstream and downstream from the specific place that you started. And so what we do in building that initial solution for the first day, we're going to need to pull in another team for the second day, and the third day, as you kind of expand fractally to capture that actual value chain. In the course of a week, you should see this come to life. If it comes to life, then what we want to do is replicate that for each and every one of your critical value chains over the course of a very intense 30-day period. In that 30-day period, we're going to be very demanding of your time, attention, and requirements in order to move at pace. If we can do that together over that 30-day period, what we'd like to exit with is a one-year plan that is going to take this system and integrate it across the entirety of your business. So at the end of 12 months, what you have in 2027 is financials that look like ours. This type of engagement is what I'm spending all of my time on. We understand that it requires a lot of engagement from you all. Of course, that requires trust and understanding in our relationship. We'll meet you where you are if you aren't there yet, to the extent that we have capacity. But I want to be very clear about everything that we're earning as we build your trust is to get into an operating model that is this aggressive, because this technology is begging to be deployed this aggressively such that you all win. Thank you.

TL;DR Under Secretary Emil Michael is pivoting the Department of Defense from "peacetime speed" to "wartime speed" by prioritizing applied AI and dismantling restrictive commercial contracts that previously gave private vendors veto power over military operations. Strategic...

TL;DR

Under Secretary Emil Michael is pivoting the Department of Defense from "peacetime speed" to "wartime speed" by prioritizing applied AI and dismantling restrictive commercial contracts that previously gave private vendors veto power over military operations.

Reforming Defense Priorities for "Wartime Speed"

  • Strategic Consolidation: Upon taking office, Michael reduced the Department’s 14 critical technology priority areas to six to eliminate "techno-babble" and focus resources on high-impact combat power.
  • Ending "Peacetime Speed": Michael argues the U.S. has operated at a "peacetime speed" since the 1990s, leading to an over-consolidated industrial base focused on dividends rather than innovation.
      • The China Challenge: While the U.S. slowed down, China engaged in the largest military buildup in history, forcing the U.S. to now aggressively re-domesticate supply chains for critical minerals, batteries, and precursors.
  • Applied AI as Priority One: The Chief Digital and AI Office was moved under Michael’s purview to accelerate deployment. In 90 days, AI adoption grew from 80,000 users to 1.2 million across the Department.

Addressing the Commercial AI "Vendor-Lock" Crisis

  • Restrictive Contractual Terms: Michael discovered "holy cow" moments in contracts written under the previous administration, where commercial AI vendors (such as Anthropic) included terms that could restrict the military from using models for kinetic strikes or sensitive operations.
  • Operational Risk: These contracts created a "vendor-lock" situation where a single company’s "corporate constitution" could theoretically turn off essential software during active combat, putting American lives at risk.
  • The Maduro Raid Incident: Following a successful military operation against Maduro's forces, a senior executive from a primary AI vendor questioned if their software was used because they were "not sure they’d like that."
      • Command and Control: Michael asserts that private company values cannot dictate U.S. command and control; the military must follow laws passed by Congress, not the ethical "souls" of private AI models.

The Three Pillars of Military AI

  • Enterprise Efficiency: Using AI for mundane corporate tasks and logistics to increase organizational speed.
  • Intelligence Processing: Leveraging decades of siloed satellite imagery and data for anomaly detection.
      • Impact: AI allows a single human analyst to increase their throughput by a factor of 1,000.
  • Warfighting and Logistics: Using AI for wargaming, operation planning, and optimizing fuel/asset movement in contested environments.

Modernizing Procurement for the Startup Ecosystem

  • Bureaucracy Reform: Michael and Secretary Pete Hegseth are moving away from 1,000-page RFPs toward "firm-fixed-price" contracts.
      • The "Elon Model": Following the SpaceX example, the Department wants to reward companies that economize and innovate, rather than using "cost-plus" contracts that incentivize delays.
  • Production vs. Invention: While startups excel at invention, Michael notes they must "cross the chasm" into manufacturing and scale to compete with major primes.
  • Cultural Shift: The Department is working to provide "faster yeses and faster nos" to startups, ensuring founders don't waste years chasing a contract that will never materialize.

Notable Quotes

"Our adversaries are stealing American models, taking the guardrails off, and potentially using those against us. Am I going to have my arm tied behind my back against the same model that has been stolen by the adversary?" — Emil Michael
"This technology... has to be our choice. It can't be... someone's soul of their model, their constitution—which is not the US Constitution—dictating our command and control environment and telling generals and warfighters what to do and not do." — Emil Michael
"Secretary Hegseth says we're on an unstoppable battle against the bureaucracy... cost-plus, endless development cycles, doesn't work. We need faster development cycles, risk sharing with industry, and clear demand signals." — Emil Michael

This is kind of like how we tell founders: you can be a wartime CEO or a peacetime CEO. What does peacetime speed mean? What is the problem with it? And what does winning look like? What does moving with urgency look like? Well, peacetime speed started after the Cold War was over at the Department of War. And I guess there was some famous event called the Last Supper, where the leaders at the Pentagon said to industry: "Hey, there's not going to be a lot more buying of weapons systems or innovation, so you all should consolidate and slow down sort of your growth, basically, and become dividend payers and stock buyback-ers." That lasted for a long time, and then the industry consolidated down to four or five primes. So then we're faced with the biggest military buildup in history in China starting in the mid-2000s, like 2010, and we didn't catch up. So all of a sudden we've outsourced a lot of our key domestic production on many different areas, whether it's critical minerals, batteries, or a lot of the supply chain stuff. And then you look up and you're like, holy cow, we've got a lot of catching up to do. So wartime speed is ensuring that we're re-domesticating the critical things we need for national strength. And we're moving out on that. We have exquisite capabilities, we have a lead in so many areas, but we have to make sure that we're self-reliant in key areas. And so we're running as hard as we can on that. You know, it was amazing to me, we work with a company called Skydio, and they were sanctioned by China. And you know, we usually hear stories about how companies are sanctioned by the United States from doing business here. But it actually, if you're a company, you're an important company, and you get sanctioned by China and you can no longer buy motors or batteries or other really key things that primarily come from China, it stinks. If those products make it into the defense procurement cycle and we can't get them anymore, it's a huge problem. How do you figure out? I mean, if you read the news, it feels like there's an endless list of things that we need to catch up on. Well, when I took the role in May, I was sworn in, did the inventory like any new leader would do, and we had 14 critical priority areas in this role. So I said, let's study and say what's really important. And we got them down to six, and they were the places where I thought we had the greatest opportunity for change and for growth and impact on our combat power and for our industrial base. And that was just the starting point. Applied AI was number one. So then we moved the Chief Digital and AI Office into my group and we were able to run extremely fast with that because we're way behind in AI at the Department. Our adversaries were also using it for lots of different purposes because they have less trust in their command and control. So they want to use computers to sort of eliminate human decision-making. Well then everyone wins, right? There'll be and the venture community sort of is very comfortable with that model, right? You bet on winners, not everyone's going to win. But cost-plus, endless development cycles, doesn't work. And so we need faster development cycles, risk sharing with industry, clear demand signals, simpler ways to do business. And those are the kind of bureaucracies that like every day I'm moving the debris out to try to make it happen. That's good, you're like regulatory Moses, that's good. So that to me is one of the most important things that's changed and it actually gets the founders fired up to build. But what is it that you need, now that the government is saying we're ready to do business, we're going to make it easier to do business, we want the best capabilities? What do you need from the companies and the startups? The one thing the primes have an advantage on is not the inventiveness. It's the production and manufacturing ability to scale what they do with what they've initially built. Because the culture there again is to never say no. And I've tried to move it to faster yeses, faster nos. So that if you're a startup, and I've felt this pain as a startup guy when you don't really know if you're getting a deal, I'd rather be told no so that I can move either onto next partner, readjust my product strategy, or do something.
